              EXTRACT FROM LETTER FROM MRS. RUSSELL A. LEWIS
                        TO MISS ANNIE GREGG




          "I am trying to locate JOHN COONER of the Revolution.  I
find a John Cooner who could be of the period mentioned in the will
of George Carter of Richland County, S.C.  Edwin L. Green in his
History of Richland County, S.C., quotes Geo. Carter mentioning his
wife Sarah, and after her death dividing his estate into two equal
shares, and go to children of Robert Carter (his brother) and
children of 'Mary wife of John Cooner Sr. -- sister of my wife to
her children....John Cooner Jr., Mary Cooner Jr., and Melchior.'
Later I had Mr. Leonardo Andrea of Columbia, S.C. send me an
abstract of George Carter Will....   Then he [Mr. Andrea] says that
it is thought that George Carter's wife Sara was a daughter of
WILLIAM FOX, also Robert Carter's wife was Unity Fox, which was true.
Mr. Andrea sent me the abstract to the two Carter wills and William
Fox.

          "Geo. Carter will was probated Sept. 24, 1804, and HENRY
FOX was executor and Mr. Andrea says 'relationship not shown but
seems a brother of his wife.'  William Fox was one of the appraisers
of the estate.

          "...You will note that illiam Fox mentions as daughter,
Mary POWELL...Mr. Andrea wondered if by 1808 date of signing of Fox
will, that Cooner had died and Mary married Powell.  He did not
mention children of Mary as she was still living, but did mention
children of deceased daughters."




    [The above is a quotation from a letter dated June 3, 1963,
    from Mrs. Russell A. Lewis, 665 E.N. 20th St., Abilene, Texas,
    to Miss Annie Gregg, also of Abilene, Texas.  Miss Gregg is a
    descendant of Henry Fox of Mississippi through his son Jacob
    Fox.  In September 1971, Mrs. Lewis told Shirley Faucette that
    she had not found additional information on this subiect.l

Exhibit 4                                                                43




                     NOTES CONCERNING SARAH CARROLL


          Practically none of the pieces of the "Carroll Puzzle" fit.
A graduate student was doing research in the library of the South
Carolina Department of Archives and History during the week we [Emma
Shirley Fox Faucette and Shirley Faucette] spent there.  He had also
tried to determine the relationships of the various Carroll families
whose wills were on file there.  His advice was to forget it.

          By some good fortune it may be possible later to untangle
the mass of Carroll families that have confused those searching for
Sarah Carroll.

          In the meantime, there are only conjectures.  Mr. Joseph E.
Steadman has raised an interesting possibility in his book, Ancestry
of the Fox Family, etc., Chapter 3, Section 1, Page 34, that Henry
Fox 2nd, who had several sons (including Col. Henry Fox of South
Carolina) also may have been the father of a daughter.  Further, this
daughter might have been named Unity, which was the name of Henry Fox
2nd's grandmother, Unity Croshaw (mother of Anne West).  Unity is a
frequent name in the descendants of William Fox, son of Col. Henry Fox,
and it also appears in the families of at least two Carroll men who
were probable contemporaries of William Fox and his wife Sarah Carroll
Fox.  The name Unity also appears in the Johnson-Hughes families.

          It is then speculated that this possible Unity Fox was married
to John Carrell, a neighbor of the Foxes in Brunswick County, Virginia,
whose land is described as adjoining 555 acres sold by Henry Fox in
Brunswick County, Virginia, in 1735.

          If they were the parents of the following named children,
including Sarah Carroll Fox, this could account for the use of the name
Unity in the families of Jacob Carrell, Sr., and William Carrell of
Richland County, South Carolina.

          The will of Jacob Carroll, Sr., was proved June 21, 1815, and
is recorded in Richland County, South Carolina.  He names a daughter
Unity who married Joseph Collins and was living in York County in the
1790 census.  Other children named are sons William, James, Jacob,
Joseph, and John.

          William Carrell, whose will was proved April 6, 1835, in
Richland County, South Carolina, also had a daughter Unity who married
--- Gray.  William Carrell also names his wife, Rebecca; daughters Rachel
who m. --- Addison, and Rebecca who m. --- Sowden, and Anna;  sons
Athu R. and Isaac.

_




                                                                                      44


            Notes Concerning Sarah Carroll (Continued)


                      It is also of record that Edward Carroll ordered 100 acres of
            land April 24, 1767, in Richland County, S.C., for William Fox, for
            which William received a grant September 4, 1770.  Edmond Carroll (the
            names appear to be used interchangeably) was also granted 100 acres on
            September 1, 1770.  Jacob Carroll was granted land in the same general
            area on September 11, 1770.

                      Edmund Carrell, Jacob Carrell, and William Fox are all listed
            in Green's History of Richland County as having Revolutionary War ser-
            vice in 1781 and following, as horsemen  at Ancrum's, Orangeburg, the
            McCord Ferry area, and other locations.  William Carrell is listed with
            service in 1781 and later, as a footman with Lt. Jacob Killingsworth, etc.
            The fact that they served together may indicate that they were about the
            same age.

                      Green's History of Richland County gives several accounts of
            military activity in this area near the homes of William Fox and the
            Carroll men.  In this connection it is interesting to note the story
            given by Mrs. Bessie Fox Truly and others [See Section IV, Exhibit 6]
            concerning Sarah Carroll Fox's encounter with a British soldier at her
            home during the Revolution.



                      The many traditional stories that Sarah Carroll was the "niece
            and adopted daughter" of Charles Carroll, are parts of the puzzle that do
            not readily fit into the Unity Fox-John Carroll theory described, unless
            additional information can be found.

                      The descendants and relations of "Charles Carroll of Carrollton"
            in Maryland are listed in many publications.  No mention is made of any
            relative named Sarah or any who had a daughter of proper age who might
            have been the wife of William Fox.

                      However, as previously mentioned, a copy of a will for a Charles
            Carroll of Charleston, South Carolina, who died in 1752, is included as
            Exhibit 6 of this section.  He refers to the "children of Martin Carroll
            by my sister Sarah of Droghadie, Kingdom of Ireland".  Apparently his
            sister Sarah Carroll married a Martin Carroll, who may have been their
            relative.  No other mention has been found of any of these Carrolls except
            Charles Carroll's daughter.         [See Exhibit 5.  Martin Carroll may
            also have been in South Carolina and may have been a son or relative of
            John Carrell of Brunswick County, Virginia, previously named, or he may
            have been in Ireland at the date of the will.

                      If William Fox were the child of Col. Henry Fox and his second
            wife, Martha Keen Fox, and was born as Mr. Steadman suggests about 1743-44,
            William Fox may have spent his early years in Charleston.  In any event,



                                                                         45



Notes Concerning Sarah Carroll (Continued)


it is likely that during his youth was the time that Col. Henry Fox is
described as being an "eminent lawyer in Charles Town" by H. T. Cook, in
his Rambles in the Pee Dee Basin.  [See Section II, Page 10]  Mr.
Steadman refers to a bill of sale signed by Col. Henry Fox of Prince
Frederick Parish, Craven County, S.C., as receiving L1000 for 20 horses
on March 6, 1745-46.  Mr. Steadman states that Col. Henry Fox moved to
or near Charleston at this time, which was when his son William was
quite young.  Col. Henry Fox is named in various records described by
Mr. Steadman which indicate that he spent most of his time at Charleston
during this period and only a part in Richland County.  It is, therefore,
possible that William Fox became acquainted with the Carroll family in
Charleston or elsewhere, prior to moving to Richland County.

          This would agree with the tradition described by Priscilla
Virginia Fox Starr [See Section IV, Exhibit l] which states that "they
Settled in South Carolina near Charleston, where all of their children
were born".  However, Mrs. Starr adds that they "were near neighbors
and friends of the late Wade Hampton's grandfather", and the Hamptons
lived in the area east of Columbia where William Fox settled later.

          All these bits of information are seemingly unrelated, except
for the likelihood, as pointed out by Mr. Steadman, that Sarah Carroll
was related in some way to Edward/Edmund Carroll and was likely a sister
of Jacob Carrell, Sr.  It is also likely that she was a sister of
William Carrell.

          Henry Fox, son of William and Sarah Carroll Fox, named his
child Joseph Carroll Fox.  It has been surmised that a daughter "Winnie
C. Fox" bore the name Carroll.

          There are at least two Joseph Carrolls who appear to be con-
temporaries of Jacob and William (besides the Joseph who was Jacob Sr.'s
son).  The various wills and estate papers associated with these Joseph
Carrolls are filled with endless names and relationships--and give not
one clue to a possible connection with the other Carrolls mentioned here.

          Joseph Carroll whose will was proved in Richland County in 1803
names his wife Martha (thought to be Swaney) and lists 11 children:
Samuel, Elizabeth, Jenet, Sarah, Joseph, John, Hannah, Henry, Elias,
Martha, and Isbell Wills.  He is thought to be that Joseph Carroll,
b. 1746, d. 1803, whose descendants' DAR records show that he was born in
Pennsylvania and died in South Carolina, and whose wife Martha Swaney was
born 1752 and died 1849.  She is likely that Martha Carroll who received
a pension for the Revolutionary service of Joseph Carroll in South Caro-
lina.  He may have been a son or grandson of the following Joseph Carroll,
or he may have been unrelated.

          It was apparently the Joseph Carrell who made his will in York
County, S.C., in May 1777, that influenced the previously mentioned stu-
dent at Columbia to give up genealogical research on the Carroll family.

                                                                         46



Notes Concerning Sarah Carroll (Continued)


          This Joseph Carrel(l)'s will was dated May 17, 1777, naming
his wife Jane Carrel and various children.  Apparently it was a codicil
which was dated January 2, 1784, naming wife Jennett Caryl, and some of
the same children previously mentioned.  On June 10, 1796, a will was
recorded for Jeannett Carrell, wife of Joseph Carrell, Senior, deceased,
with five then surviving children, including a son and grandson "Joseph".
The grandson Joseph was the son of Thomas Carrell.

          It could not be determined whether it was the son, grandson,
or another Joseph Carrell whose estate was apparently not settled, in
spite of many legal proceedings for closing the estate by various persons,
as late as March 1823.  Joseph Bates, John Bates [see Scott history] and
William Fair were serving as executors; Jacob and John Carroll seem to
have been administrators, and "5 heirs entitled to a distributive share"
were mentioned.  At that date names appearing in the estate records
included Esther Carrell, Dorcas Carrell, William Scott, Killingsworth,
Peter Keen, William B. Creber (husband of Esther Carrell, deceased) and
Hiram Addison (husband of Dorcas Carrell).  One of the participants in
the litigation was alleged to have "taken up with another man and left
under cover of night".  Fortunately, this was not our Sarah!

          Wills for other members of Joseph Carrell Sr.'s family were
recorded, and at least one other Joseph Carroll is named, the son of
Thomas Carrell.  Thomas Carrell named Esther Carrell and Thomas Carrell
as "grandchildren" of his "son Joseph Carrell'l.



          In summary, it is not known how Sarah Carroll was related to
any of the Carrolls mentioned herein.

          It is possible that she had brothers Jacob (Sr.), William,
Edmund/Edward, and some admired relative named Joseph Carroll and another
named Charles Carroll.  She may have been related in some way to John
Carroll of Brunswick County, Virginia.

          She was "Grandpappy" Henry Fox's mother . . .  One tradition
says she and/or her twin sons were red-headed (the Carroll's were from
Ireland) . . .  Her twin sons were 21 years younger than her oldest
known child, "Grandpappy" . . . She picked up a British soldier bodily
who tried to steal butter from her churn . . .  Her descendants described
her as "strongand resolute" . . . .

          How does one "fnret" a familv like t_hP arrolls!

                      WILL OF CHARLES CARROLL


(Will dated April 23, 1752, proved July 10, 1752; recorded in Will
Book 1747-52, page 533, Charleston, South Carolina.)


In the name of God Amen I Charles Carroll of Charles Town in the province
of South Carolina Peruke maker being sick & weak in body but of sound
perfect mind memory & understanding praised be God for the same do make                    1
Publish & Declare this my Last will & Testament in manner & form follow
ing that is to say I will that all my just debts & funeral Expenses be
duly paid & Satisfied. Item. I give unto my Loving Wife Ester Carrol
the use only of all my real & Personall Estate for the use of herself &
for the support maintenance & Education of my Daughter Henrietta Carrol,
during the Natural Life of my said Wife Ester and after her Decease I
give Devise & Bequeath unto my sd Daughter Henrietta & to her heirs &
assigns forever all my real Estate also I give unto my said Daughter
Henrietta after the Decease of my said wife Esther all my Personall
Estate. Item. I will Authorize & Impower my said wife to grant bar-
gain sell & convey to any person or persons absolutely forever all that
my Plantation or tract of land Containing five hundred Acres at Winyaw
& I will that the monies arising by the sale thereof be put out to
Interest on good Security for the use of my sd wife & the support & main-
tenance of my sd Daughter also I will authorize & Impower my said wife
to sell barter & Exchange as she shall think fitt all & any of my negro
slaves or their Issue & Increase & the monies arising by such sale                        :
barter or Exchange to put out at Interest on good Security or otherwise
Impower the same for the benefit of my sd Daughter Item. I give unto
my said Daughter after the decease of my sd wife all the monies bonds                     €   L
notes Bills & other Securities there may be received had or taken for
sale of the said Plantation or for sale barter or Exchange of all or
any of my negro slaves aforesaid & I do hereby nominate & appoint my
said wife Esther Exrix & Mr William Glenn Exor of this my Last will
Testament & Guardians Jointly & Severally over the person & Estate of
my said Daughter & after the Decease of my said wife I will authorize
& Impower the said William Glen to make sale of the above mentioned
 Plantation & negroes for the benefit of my said Daughter in the same
manner as my said wife might or could do by Virtue of the power &
 authority above given & I also will that in case my said wife die before
my sd Daughter attain the age of Eighteen years that all the Interest &                   ;
Estate by this my last will Intended for my sd Daugr shall be paid &
 Discharged Delivered over to her at the age of Eighteen or day of
 marriage and I hereby Revoke all former and other will and wills by me
 at any time heretofore made declaring this only to be my Last will
 and Testament also I will that in case my Daughter die before the age      ;
of Eighteen years or day of marriage & upon the Decease of my said
 wife I give Devise & Bequeath unto the children of Martin Carrol
 by my Sister Sarah of Droghadie in the Kingdom of Ireland & to their

                    HENRY FOX OF MISSISSIPPI


          Henry Fox is thought to have been born in Richland County,
South Carolina, on June 12, 1768, and he may have been the oldest child
of William and Sarah Carroll Fox.  He died January 18, 1852, in Webster
County, Mississippi.

          In South Carolina, Henry Fox settled on or near his father's
place in Richland County, and he married Sarah Harrell, also thought
to be from Richland County.  The date of marriage of Henry Fox to Sarah
Harrell is not known, but it was presumably in the late 1780's or 1790,
as their family Bible shows "William, first born of Henry and Sarah Fox,
born March 6, 1791".

          As discussed in Section I of this "Collection of Fox Data",
Sarah Harrell is thought to have been the daughter or granddaughter of
John or William Harrell who married in North Carolina in the mid 1700's
to Unity Fox of North Carolina.  It is speculated that this Unity Fox
was the "Noona" shown as a daughter of William and Sarah Avent Fox of
Brunswick County, Virginia, in William Fox's will.

          Henry and Sarah Fox remained in Richland County, South Carolina,
until about 1814, when they, along with their children, their children's
families, and other relatives, moved to Cocke County in East Tennessee.
All of their children, except possibly the youngest daughter, Dorcas,
were born in South Carolina, and several of them had married and had
children before leaving South Carolina.

          When Henry Fox emigrated from South Carolina to Tennessee,
the group included his children and their families, the Wooten family
(Henry's son William married Priscilla Wooten, and another son, John,
married Rebecca Wooten, sister of Priscilla); Mrs. Ann (Nancy) Johnson,
the widow of Benjamin Johnson, and her children Unity Johnson Brent,
Elizabeth Johnson Brent (who later married Henry's son Thomas Fox),
Dorcas Johnson Hughes (wife of Jesse Hughes) and family, Joseph Johnson,
and John C. Johnson; and possibly others.

          As discussed in Section I, Benjamin Johnson is speculated to
have been the son of Benjamin Johnson and Mary Claiborne Fox, who was
another daughter of William and Sarah Avent Fox.  An abstract of the
will of Benjamin Johnson and a copy of Ann Johnson's will are included
as Exhibit 13 of this section.

          All these families remained in Tennessee a few years, and it
was during this time that Henry's father, William Fox, died in South
Carolina about 1816.  Henry evidently returned to Richland County,
South Carolina, and executed a document December 6, 1816, renouncing
his executorship of his father's will, stating:

    "I am now living over the mountains and hath for some time lived
    there, so that it is Quite Inconvenient for me to act in behalf
    of his property...."

                                                                          57


        A copy of this document is included in Section III, Exhibit 1.
Mr. Z. F. Shelton, of Montgomery, Alabama, a descendant of Henry's son
Jacob Fox, called attention to the fact that Henry's location in Cocke
County, Tennessee, was just over the highest hump in the Great Smoky
Mountains, "...and he was not joking when he said I am now living over
the mountains!"  It is said that this area was very rough country and
that after seeing a fight between two women at a public gathering,
Henry Fox moved to Tuscaloosa, Alabama, probably about 1819.

        Alabama records indicate many marriages, land transactions,
census records, etc., for the numerous family groups that settled in
Alabama.  Z. F. Shelton has stated that records are on file for the sale
by William and Priscilla Fox of land in Tuscaloosa on June 27, 1832.
William and Priscilla Fox's oldest son, Malachi Fox, and his wife Ann
(Nancy) Hughes Fox, are also shown as selling land to Malachi's grand-
father, Henry Fox, in Tuscaloosa County on November 23, 1833.  These
transactions may indicate that William and Malachi sold their land
about the time they came to Mississippi and that William's father, Henry
Fox, remained in Alabama a short time longer before moving to Mississippi.
James C. Fox of Monticello, Arkansas, secured the following description of
property owned by Henry Fox in Tuscaloosa County:  "West half of Northwest
quarter of Section 7, Township 20, Range II West."  On December 8, 1957,
James C. Fox stated that this land was owned by Mrs. E. S. Price, and was
reached by turning north a few miles west of Tuscaloosa on Moone's Bridge
Road.

           It is traditional in the family of Malachi Fox, son of William
and Priscilla Wooten Fox, that William was the first Mayor of Tuscaloosa,
Alabama.  A further tradition is that William Fox was responsible for set-
ting out the large oaks which now line many streets of Tuscaloosa.  An
interesting account of early Tuscaloosa is contained in Matthew William
Clinton's Tuscaloosa, Alabama--Its Early Days 1816-1865, published in
1958.  He states that its earliest records of a mayor are in 1828, but
names of previous city officials are not known.  There are indications of
town meetings as early as January 1820.  Similarly, the records are incom-
plete regarding the planting of the oak trees in the city, but he states
"The accounts agree that the planting of oaks was begun in the 1830's and
that the city government, in some way, promoted the project."  It is also
said that William Fox once had a contract to set out evergreens "in a town
that is now called Evergreen, Alabama".  Evergreen, Alabama, is the county
seat of Conecuh County, Alabama, in southwestern Alabama.  William's brother,
John Fox who married Rebecca Wooten sister of William's wife) is said to
have settled "with his wife's people" in either Wilcox or Conecuh County.
No record has been located in Wilcox County, but an early census record
lists a Dr. Cunningham in Conecuh County who listed "Mary Fox" as a member
of the household.  John Fox's daughter is said to have married "a Dr.
Cunningham".

           About 1835, at approximately 67 years of age, Henry and Sarah
Fox and several children and their families moved to Choctaw County, Miss.,
formed 1833, and settled in that portion which is now Webster County near
Slate Springs.  His oldest son William had left Alabama earlier and settled
at DeKalb, Miss.  Sons John and Jacob and daughter Dorcas Fox Mills remained
in Alabama, but the remaining children settled near Henry, and Jacob came to
Mississippi about 1866.  See Exhibit 12 for information on Dorcas Fox Mills.

_




                                                                                      58




                      Henry Fox, his wife, and many of his family were charter members
            of Bethany Baptist Church, organized near Slate Springs in 1836, and he
            and several sons were among the first deacons, and were prominent members,
            as shown by minutes of this church.  [Minutes of this church are on file
            in the archives of the Mississippi Baptist Historical Commission, located
            at Mississippi College, Clinton, Mississippi.  Much information of
            interest to the Fox Family was copied from these minutes by Mrs. Emma
            Shirley Fox Faucette for inclusion in previous collections of information
            prepared for the Fox Family Reunion group.]

                      Henry Fox, his wife, Sarah Harrell Fox, and several of their
            children are buried in the family cemetery on his homeplace near Slate
            Springs, Mississippi.  A historical marker was placed at the site in 1960
            by members of the annual Fox Family Reunion in Missisissippi.  For the
            text of this marker, see Exhibit 14 of this section.

                      James C. Fox, in his booklet "Trailing the Foxes", written
            during the 1950's, states:

                "The tradition comes to this writer that he [Henry came to be
                called 'Grandpappy Henry' late in life, being as he was, sire
                of all the Fox family of and near Slate Springs, Miss.,...with
                wife, four sons, two daughters, their families and perhaps several
                families of friends as they were among the very first settlers in
                that part of the state."  [Excerpts from "Trailing the Foxes" are
                included in Exhibit 8 of this section.]

                      In Exhibitlis a letter written in 1902 by Priscilla Virginia
           Fox Starr (1836-1913), great-granddaughter of Henry and Sarah Harrell Fox.
           In this letter, Mrs. Starr recounts stories and traditions she remembers
           hearing from her grandfather, William Fox, oldest son of Henry and Sarah
           Fox, and from her father, Malachi Fox, the oldest son of his father.  In
           addition, Mrs. Starr comments on the double relationships in her family:
           her mother, Ann (ancy) Hughes Fox, was a daughter of Jesse and Dorcas
           Johnson Hughes, and a sister of other Hughes daughters who married Fox
           men; her grandmother, Priscilla Wooten, wife of William Fox, was a sister
           of Rebecca Wooten who married William's brother, John Fox. She says:

                "When our great-grandfather emigrated from South Carolina to Alabama,
                our grandfather William had married and had one or two children
                (maybe three), the oldest being our own dear father [Malachi Fox].
                His mother's family [Priscilla Wooten Fox] and my mother's family
                [Ann (Nancy) Hughes Fox] who were S.C. neighbors and relations, all
                came to Alabama a' the same time.  Was not that a mighty caravan,
                with their covered wagons and negroes, etc., such as I have often
                seen going to La., Ark., and Texas, but as you have never seen as
                you are such a 'fin de siecle' boy.

                "In the course of events our great-grandfather moved up to what was
                then the line between Choctaw and Grenada Counties in this state -



_




                                                                                      59




                most of his children went with him.  His son Jacob remained in
                Alabama, his son William came to Kemper County, Mississippi.  My
                mother's parents [Jesse and Dorcas Johnson Hughes] also came to
                Choctaw County, which is now Webster county or just on the line
                between what is now Webster and Calhoun...."


                      It is of interest to note the comments of Mr. Joseph E.
            Steadman given on page 57 of his book, concerning the South Carolina
            1790 census not having been completed until February 1792.  Henry Fox
            is shown in this census for Camden District, Richland County, South
            Carolina, as head of a household having one male over 16, one male
            under 16, and one female.  This census was evidently made subsequent
            to the birth of their son William, born March 6, 1791, recorded in the
            family Bible as "William, first born of Henry and Sarah Fox, born
            March 6, 1791".

                      The microfilm of the 1810 Richland County Census checked by this
            writer was difficult to read, but it showed that Henry Fox then had
            listed a household composed of one or two males under 10, 2 males
            aged 10 to 16, 2 males aged 16 to 26, and one male 45 and up; 3 females
            under 10, and 1 female 45 and up; and 2 slaves, totaling 12 or 13.

                      The thirteen children of Henry and Sarah Harrell Fox,
            together with their children, where known, are included as Exhibit 15
            of this section.

                      At one time, there was a mistaken impression that William Fox
            who married Priscilla Wooten was a brother of Henry Fox who married
            Sarah Harrell, rather than their son.  A letter dated December 4, 1904,
            from Carl Fox (1876-1958) to Eleanor Fox Ponder [See Exhibit 3]
            gave information about the Fox family as he recalled it from discussions
            with his father, Henry (Hally) Fox, who was a grandson of Henry and
            Sarah Fox.  He stated:

                "William Cof Richland County, South Carolina, who married Sarah
                Carroll] had a son Henry, who was called Hal or Hally, my great-
                grandfather, and a son William who was Mrs. Starr's grandfather....
                I do not know whether William (the first) and his wife had any
                other children besides Hally and William (the second) or not....
                You will notice that Mrs. Starr has it that William II, your
                great-grandfather and Hally II, my great-grandfather, were the
                brothers of my grandfather Thomas...; but this is a mistake....

                I'I must not forget to say that it is possible that I am mistaken
                in saying that your great-grandfather William was a brother of my
                great-grandfather Hally.  It may be that William, your great-
                grandfather was a son of Hally, my great-grandfather."

                      The latter paragraph is correct, rather than the preceding
             information, and Mrs. Starr was correct in her statement.  This is included
             here in order to clear up any misunderstanding that might result from
             extant copies of the 1904 letter.



_





             Exhibit 1                                                                 60





                          LETTER FROM PRISCILLA VIRGINIA FOX STARR
                            TO HER HALF-BROTHER, JAMES ISRAEL FO


                                                         Fannin (Miss.), June 6, 1902

             Dear Jim,

                       I received your letter yesterday and was glad to hear from you
             and am real proud of your children and of your apparent prosperity.

                       Now concerning the Fox Clan, I fear that I can give you but
             little satisfactory information, but will tell you what I know and have
             heard.  When I was about eighteen years old I spent a year with my
             mother's parents, who were related to these Foxes at what is now known
             as Slate Springs, about 30 miles from Grenada.  Then I have heard of
             many old family traditions from my dear old Grandfather Fox, who liked
             to talk of those things to us youngsters, up there at old Bethany Church,
             near our great-grandfather's plantation.  I've often seen the brick tomb
             that then (47 years ago) covered our great grandparents.  All around
             them were many graves of their descendants, most of these graves being
             short ones for I think all of their children were then living.  I had
             been told that the first Fox's who came to this country were two twin
             brothers, William and Henry, who settled in Virginia.  After a while one
             of them went to Maryland, and after another little while the one left in
             Virginia or his son, went to Maryland to visit his brother, or uncle,
             and got his wife there, a Miss Carroll, a niece or grandniece of "Charles
             Carroll of Carrollton", one of the signers of the Declaration of Indepen-
             dence.  He returned to his home in Virginia.  This couple was our great
             grandfather and mother.*  They settled in South Carolina near Charleston,
             where all of their children were born - were near neighbors and friends
             of the late Wade Hampton's grandfather.  Their children whom I have known
             were William (our grandfather), John (who married a sister of our grand-
             mother), Joseph, Henry (who was called Hallie or Hal), Jacob (who married
             my mother's oldest sister), Thomas (who was Fuller's and Manly's grand-
             father), James (the preacher).  The daughters were Unity (who married a
             Pryor) and Polly (who married a Denton).  These were my father's uncles
             and aunts that I know, and I think all of them.  When our great grand-
             father emigrated from Soutn Carolina to Alabama, our grandfather, William,
             had married and had one or two children (maybe three), the oldest being
             our own dear father.  His mother's famiiy and my mother's family who were
             S.C. neighbors and relations all came to Alabama at the same time.  Was
             not that a mighty caravan, with their covered wagons and negroes, etc.,
             such as I have often seen going to La., Ark. and Texas, but as you have
             never seen, as you are such a "fin de siecle" boy!




             *[Note by Shirley Faucette:  William and Sarah Carroll Fox were her
             great-great grandfather and grandmother.  Her great-grandparents were
             Henry and Sarah Harrell Fox.]

_




             (Letter from Priscilla Virginia Fox Starr - Continued)                   61


                     In the course of events our great-grandfather moved up to what
             was then the line between Choctaw and Grenada Counties in this state -
             most of his children went with him.  His son Jacob remained in Ala.
             His son William came to Kemper County, Miss.  My mother's parents with
             their children also came to Choctaw County, which is now Webster County,
             or just on the line between what is now Webster and Calhoun.  When I was
             up there they had become a host and lived in what is now the corners of
             four different counties.

                     Old Uncle John finally moved to Wilcox County, Ala. with his
             wife's folks - had one child, a daughter.  He, Uncle John, finally died
             a very wealthy man.  His daughter married a Dr. Cunningham.  I've heard
             nothing of them since the war.  In my young days they often visited my
             grandparents at DeKalb.  You see they were two brothers who married
             sisters.  It had always been the custom in the Fox family to name two of
             their sons William and Henry, until our father had to be named, and his
             grandfather insisted that he be named Malachi, so our grandfather had no
             William and Henry, but my mother had twin sons who were named William
             and Henry, neither of whom survived at the age of maturity, but when our
             own Will came his father insisted he be named William.

                     Well excuse my many digressions, but as I write these many little
             memories crowd on my mind and I get on things "furrin to the issue".

                     Now as to Fuller's immediate family, his father was a son of old
             Uncle Tommie.  Uncle Tommie married my mother's own aunt.  His son Henry
             or Hallie, married my mother's sister - so you see they were own cousins.
             Their older children, Manly, Fuller, Nannie, etc., were young folks with
             me and I was much attached to them.  They were very bright and quick
             intellectually.  There were several younger ones, for my uncle and aunt
             had a house full it seems to me, but they were younger than I, and I
             don't remember anything of the one you call Carl.  He may be a son of
             Uncle Hallie's second wife, for my Aunt Sarah died many years ago.
             Manly, the oldest son, died many years ago - taken in Mobile while study-
             ing medicine.  I corresponded with Nannie until the last few years.  I
             much regret that we ever gave up the correspondence, and if you get her
             address from her brother I would be so glad.  One of the sisters, Emma,
             I think, married a missionary.  You see these children of Uncle Hallie's
             are my own cousins through my mother, while they are yours and my second
             cousins through their father.

                     Our pastor here last year (a Mr. Allen - a student at Clinton and
             a great grandson of one of our father's uncles or aunts - I never could
             find out exactly which one) has told me a good deal about the family as
             he knows them, but seemed to know but little about those I know.  Our
             present pastor, Mr. Shepherd, also a student at Clinton, comes from the
             Fox country, and from what he tells me the younger generations are worthy
             descendants of noble sires.  As I knew them, they were people who were
             independent in worldly affairs, sober, honest, walked uprightly before
             God and man, thought the Fox family the cream of the human family - never
             dreamed of toadying to any man or asking any man, except a Fox, his
             opinion as to how they should act in any doubtful emergency.

(Letter from Priscilla Virginia Fox Starr - Continued)                    62


        I have certainly written a disconnected scratch and my memory
may have played me false in some things, but remember what my memory
has to carry since these long past days when we young Foxes went to
old Bethany Church where our Uncle Jimmie preached, or when others of
us sat about dear old grandfather's knees and heard him repeat many
charming tales of "ye olden times".

        Give my love to your dear family.  If I can give you any more
desired information, or make plainer anything I may have written, let
me know.  Many thanks for your kind invitation.  I may avail myself of
it some day.  My health is better than in the winter.  A letter from
you now and then would be a great kindness to me.

        Always your affectionate sister,


                                       P. V. Starr.

Nannie wrote me yesterday that Tom
had struck oil.






[This letter typed by Shirley Faucette from handwritten copy made by
Mrs. Blondie Fox Richardson, a sister of Carl Fox named in this letter.
Copies were made by several other persons, including one made by John
Henry Fox, Jr., which is essentially the same as this, but omits the
post script.]



 Exhibit 2                                                                                                          63



                  LETTER FROM CARL FOX TO LEE FOX


                                                       Vicksburg, Miss.
                                                       Aug. 18, 1902

Dear Lee:-

       Since coming to Vicksburg I have become acquainted with a dis-
tant cousin who is station agent at Bovina, a little place ten miles
from Vicksburg on the A & V Road.  I went out to see him one Sunday
and tried to trace our relationship, but he knew little about his branch
of the family because he left home when only about fifteen years old.
He became very much interested in the family history and afterward wrote
to an older sister, (half sister) who lives at Fannin in Rankin County
asking for information about the family, and two or three days ago he
handed me the letter he received from her in reply.

       As you are, up to this time, the only one in our family who have
any boys to continue the name of Fox, I enclose you a copy of this let-
ter, for it is full of information about the family that I know you will
want to hand down to William and Hally.  I already knew all that she
tells in her letter, for I have talked to Pa a great deal about the
family history.  He wrote it all out once, but the papers were lost in
some way.  He remembered very distinctly - in fact he was grown - when
his grandfather Henry (Hally) died.  His grandfather was a grandson of
the Henry (called Hally) who first came from England and settled in
Virginia, and he of course knew all about his father and grandfather,
and from him Pa learned about them.  Mrs. Starr never knew this grand-
father of Pa's, her great grandfather, for she speaks of his tomb being
there at the time of her visit to our people at Slate Springs, and she
has part of the history wrong.  This Hally Fox, our and her great grand-
father, was not a son, as she says, of the one who came from England,
but he was a grandson.  Here is the line of descent:

Letter from Carl Fox to Lee Fox (Continued)                              64


        William Fox, son of Hally, the First, was the one who went to
Maryland and married a niece of Charles Carroll, whom Mrs. Starr
speaks of.  This Miss Carroll, Mrs. William Fox then, was a very large,
strong woman, and once threw a Tory soldier bodily out of her house
during the Revolutionary War.  Her husband, William, fought under
General Marion.  The tale is authentic, for Pa got it from his grand-
father, who was her son.

        William Fox died in South Carolina, and his son Henry, the
Second, emigrated to Tennessee, and afterwards to Alabama then to old
Choctaw County, Mississippi, where he died.  I suppose Nan has seen
his tomb on Cousin Joe Pryor's place.  If he had been the son of the
first Henry, as Mrs. Starr says, he would have been an hundred years
old at the time of his death, which only occurred fifty-two or three
years, if that long, ago.

        You have two boys named William and Hally, as were the twins
who came from England.  Did you know Pa's name was Henry and that he
changed it to Hally after he was grown to avoid confusion with all the
other numerous Henry Fox's.  He was always called Hally anyway.  I do
not know whether you will be particularly interested in this or not,
but on account of your boys thought perhaps you might be.

                                Your brother,

                                Carl Fox.

 Exhibit 3                                                                65




            LETTER FROM CARL FOX TO ELEANOR FOX (PONDER)


                                                          [Vicksburg, Miss.]
                                                          Dec. 4, 1904

 My dear cousin Eleanor:-

         I received your letter nearly two weeks ago and, I would have
 answered it at once, had it not been that I was very busy in Circuit
 Court and did not have time to giveitthe answer it deserved.  I confess
 with shame, my discourtesy in not returning your copy of the pedigree
 of the Fox family, from Burke's Peerage, but since it has been the
 occasion of a very interesting letter from my cousin and one that I
 appreciate highly, I must also confess that the edge is taken off of my
 penitence.  I return the copy with this letter, having made a copy for
 myself.  I also enclose a typewritten copy of a letter from your Aunt,
 Mrs. Starr, written to cousin Jim Fox, at Bovina.  My father always was
 interested in tracing our family pedigree, and when he was a young man,
 learned a great deal about it from his grandfather Henry (called Hally)
 Fox.  And I have often talked with my father, before his death, about
 it, so I suppose I do know more of the family history than any other
 member of my branch of the family.

         Mrs. Starr's letter has a great deal of very interesting infor-
 mation, but beyond her grandfather William, she has the pedigree mixed.*
 Here it is as given me by my father, who got it from his grandfather,
 who was a grandson of one of the brothers who came over from England to
 Virginia.

         Some time before the Revolution (how long before I do not know)
 two brothers William and Henry (called Hal or Hally) came from England
 to Virginia.  We are descended from Henry or Hally, as he was always
 called.  Henry had a son William, named for his uncle, I suppose, who
 married a niece or daughter of Charles Carroll, as stated by your aunt
 Mrs. Starr, and moved with his family to South Carolina, before the
 Revolution, living in or near the conjunction of the Congaree and Wateree
 Rivers.  He may have lived near Charleston at one time, as your aunt
 states.  He was a young man, or at least comparatively speaking, during
 the Revolution and fought under Gen. Marion.  His wife, a very large and
 powerful woman, once threw a tory soldier, who insisted in putting his
 hand in a newly churned jar of milk after the butter, bodily out of her
 house.  She had offered to give him a glass of the buttermilk after she
 had taken out the butter.  The tradition is that the officer in command
 drove the fellow away with jeers, telling him that he was not half a man.
 William's wife was a widow when he married her, with two sons - I think
 twins - named Elisha and Elijah and they were very wild and dissolute
 and spent everything their mother and William owned.*

*[Note by Shirley Faucette:  Some of these statements are incorrect, as he
 states, and reference should be made to correct information contained
 elsewhere in this collection.]



                                                                         66




Letter from Carl Fox to Eleanor Fox (Ponder) - (Continued)


          William and his wife had a son named Henry, who was called Hal
or Hally, my great-grandfather, and a son William who was Mrs. Starr's*
grandfather, and if your father and Mrs. Starr are brother and sister,
your great-grandfather.

          I do not know whether William (the first) and his wife had
any other children besides Hally and William (the second) or not.  I do
not know where William II. lived.  Hally II moved when a young man, from
South Carolina to Cocke County, Tennessee, but did not live there very
long.  While he was living there he saw two women who had quarrelled, have
a rough and tumble fight, while a crowd formed a ring and cheered.  This
disgusted him so that he packed up and moved to Alabama, Pickens County.
Hally II was married and had grown children when he moved to Alabama and
on the trip from Tennessee, which of course was made in wagons and horse-
back, my grandfather, Thomas Fox, who was Hally II's son, married his
cousin, on the way to Alabama*, who was a young widow with one child, a
boy.  She was the widow Brent or Brant, but her maiden name was Johnson.

          Hally II's other children were John, Joseph, Jacob, James,
Unity, Polly and David.  There may have been others but they are all I
can recall.*

          You will notice that Mrs. Starr has it that William II, your
great-grandfather and Hally II, my great-grandfather, were the brothers
of my grandfather Thomas, James, Joseph, John, Jacob, David, Unity and
Polly, but this is a mistake.* Hally II was their father and William II
was their uncle.*  My grandfather Thomas' children were John, Joseph,
Thomas and Henry, (always called Hally) who was my father.  Thus my father
was the third Hally in a direct line,* since the family came to America.
There are so many of the same name that it is very confusing.  For
instance, my father and his brothers, except uncle Tom, were all given
the names of their uncles, and uncle Tom was named after his father, my
grandfather.  With a little study, however you can get it all straight.

          I cannot tell you who were the wives of all my uncles and their
children, for it would take a week.  My father first married his first
cousin who was a Hughes and their children are Fuller, who lives at West
Point, [Miss.] Nannie, who is now Mrs. R. H. Conner and, Dee (Delilah
originally, I think) now Mrs. Davis who both live at Wordwell, in Calhoun
County, Miss., and both have grandchildren.  Emmeline, who is Mrs. Puthuff
and lives in Texas and Jesse, who was killed at the Battle of Shiloh when
he was twenty or twenty-one years of age; Manly who died a few years
after the war when he was about grown.  Their mother died before the war
and my father married my mother soon after the war.

          My mother was a widow McCord, [Tabitha Angeline Cooke McCord],
with two children, Jimmie and Virgie, when she and my father were married.
So you see, I have half brothers and sisters on both sides.  Jimmie is
now Mrs. James Pryor, with a married daughter and five other children,
two of these nearly grown.  Her husband, by the way is a grandson of my
grandfather's sister Unity, who married a Pryor.  My half sister Virgie

                                                                         67
Letter from Carl Fox to Eleanor Fox (Ponder) - (Continued)


married a Mr. James Smith and she died about a year ago.  My full
brothers and sisters are Jesse, professor of Mathematics at the A. & M.
College, Dr. Lee T. Fox, who lives at Water Valley.  You asked me about
him in your letter.  He is next older than I, but there was a sister
Grace, between us, who died when she was only a year or two old.  Then
comes myself, then Bessie, now Mrs. Ben Truly, living at Fayette, Miss.
Then Hally the youngest who is now in his third year at the United
States Military Academy at West Point, N.Y.  Jesse, Lee and Bess are
all married.

        My letter has grown very voluminous, although I suspect I have
left out many things you would like to know.  I have tried to give an
outline of the family history, and if there is anything you would like
to hear in more detail, and I can tell you let me know and I will be
glad to do it.  Of your branch of the family I know very little, although
I have often heard my father and uncles speak of different members of
your branch.

        Your aunt, Mrs. Starr can give you a complete history I have no
doubt.  Your grandfather, Mala Fox's name is very familiar to me.  From
your description of him, he was a true Fox, as I have known them.  They
are all high tempered, all very fond of reading, and all lazy, and since
I am talking to one of the Fox's, I will say that they are the brightest
and have the best judgements of any family of people I ever knew.  That
sounds egotistical perhaps, but it is a fact nevertheless.

        I must not forget to say that it is possible that I am mistaken
in saying that your great-grandfather William was a brother of my great-
grandfather Hally.  It may be that William, your great-grandfather was
a son of Hally, my great-grandfather. [true]  I wish you would write to
Mrs. Starr, or find out from your father.  Mrs. Starr speaks of her
great-grandfather Fox at Slate Springs, and of the brick tomb there of
her great-grandparents.  And the brick tomb she speaks of is that of my
great-grandfather Hally.  On the other hand, I don't remember ever hear-
ing that my grandfather had a brother named William.  I am going to write
to my folks and find out about it.  When we get this information, perhaps
we can figure out exactly what relation we are.

        Cousin Jim Fox at Bovina looks a great deal like my brothers, Lee
and Fuller.  I was mighty glad you sent me your picture and I think you
and my sister Bessie would look very much alike to a stranger, but you
know that one cannot see the family resemblance of his own people as well
as strangers.  I am sorry that I have no photograph of myself to send you.
Mine would not be a fair exchange for yours anyway; I would still be in
your debt.  Your cousin Albert is not so very anxious to get the picture
back, is he?  He can see the original of it and I cannot and I think I
shall keep the picture - at least until I am sternly commanded to return
it forthwith.  You are nearly as large as I am.  I am five feet ten
inches in height and weigh about one hundred and fifty pounds.  My eyes
are blue, my hair is brown, that is where it is not too thin to discern
the color.  I also am a great believer in plenty of exercise and have

                                                                          68
(Letter from Carl Fox to Eleanor Fox (Ponder) - (Continued)


been taking it in allopathic doses all my life, hunting, fishing,
football when in school, running, riding, rowing and swimming but I
am too busy now to keep it up, very regularly.  So I think we would
be very congenial.

        And now my cousin, I am going to close this little note and
next time perhaps I will write you a long letter.  I hope we will
meet sometime soon, when it will not be so tedious to trace our
kinship as it is with a slow pen.


                                                                Your Cousin,

                                     Carl Fox.

Dear Jimmy:

           Your letter of October 31 came in due time.

           I have had mimeographed and enclose a copy of my sister's
compilation of what knowledge she was able to obtain many years ago
about the Fox family, preceded by a preface that I have written
since receiving your letter.

           You will see on page 10 of my sister's compilation that
Henry Fox, the fourth child, born in 1798, of Henry Fox (the first
in Mississippi) and his wife Sarah, was the grandfather of your
Uncle Jerry Fox. She was not able to obtain full information about
his family and does not mention your father, but I am sure that what
she did obtain was correct.  And by the way, you do not state your
father's given name in your letter.  I would like to know it and
mention him at the proper place in my sister's compilation.

           I am glad to hear that you and Dot Mallory found the graves
of my great grandfather and your great great grandfather Henry Fox
and his wife Sarah.  In 1911 I was at Slate Springs and tried to find
their graves but was unable to locate them.  I would be glad to share
in the expense of the granite marker that you and Dot have arranged
for.  You refer to Dot as "Aunt Dot".  As a matter of fact she is
your cousin, being the daughter of my father's youngest brother, Dr.
Thomas Fox.  I do not know her Post Office address.  I have an impres-
sion that she and her husband Verna Mallory moved away from Slate
Springs to some other part of Calhoun County many years ago.  I want
to send her a copy of this genealogy, and shall be obliged if you will
let me know what her present address is.

           When I have more time I will add quite a number of descen-
dants of the first Henry Fox in Mississippi born since my sister's
compilation was made.

           I wonder if you have ever met Manly Fox, who is a grandson of
my father's brother John J. Fox born June 2, 1818, and who was my first
cousin on our mother's side.  His father was Sam Fox, so that he is

                                      LETTER FROM CARL FOX, MOBILE, ALA3AMA,
                      TO MRS. DOT FOX MALLORY, CALHOUN CITY, MISS.


                                                              17 December 1954,
                                                                CF:sfs.


            Dear Dot:

                      I was delighted to get your letter of December 14th.  I
            enclose herewith a copy of the Fox genealogy, copies of which I have
            sent to "Jimmie" (James C.) Fox at Monticello, Arkansas, to one of
            our cousins, Mrs. Jimmie V. Phillips, at Slate Springs, and to
            another cousin, Z. F. Shelton, 1820 Graham Street, Montgomery 6,
            Alabama.  In December of last year Jimmie and Z. F. Shelton wrote
            Carl, Jr. at Jackson, Mississippi, under the impression that it
            was I that lived there, and some months ago I had a letter from
            Cousin Jimmie Phillips.  I had never heard of any of the three
            cousins, but have had some pleasant correspondence with them.

                      In 1911 I was at Slate Springs and spent the night with
            Cousin Jim Pryor, whose mother, as you know, was Unity Fox, our
            grandfather's sister.  He was then nearly 75 years of age, and was
            15 or 16 years of age when our great grandfather Henry Fox died in
            1852.  He had visited his grandfather and grandmother many times
            and told me many interesting things about them - among others that
            he had many slaves, the increase of a few that he bought or inherited
            when he was a young man.  Cousin Jim said that he had no interest in
            making money and that his slaves were a burden rather than a source of
            profit.  He never sold one except with his or her consent - usually
            when one married the slave of someone else, and that sometimes he
            would buy the husband or wife from his or her owner in order that
            they might be together.  Cousin Jim told me that he was not sure where
            our great grandfather and great grandmother were buried.  We tried to
            locate the graves, but were unable to do so.  I have had it in mind
            for a good many years to put a tombstone somewhere in the grave yard,
            even if I could not locate the graves, but never did get around to it.
            I am very much pleased that you and Jimmie Fox have done so.

                      Necessarily there was some incidental expenses in addition
            to the actual cost of the stone, $73.44.  Assuming that the total
            expense must have been at least $80.00, I enclose my check for $13.35,
            and I am sending a check for the same amount to Jimmie.  That is on
            the assumption also that the expense was divided equally between you.
            It is a pleasure to share in the cost.

                      Lee will be 85 years of age on his next birthday, May 7.  He
            actively practiced medicine until about three years ago.  He cannot
            see well enough to read, but he could still get about a little if it
            were not for his artificial leg.  He spends the daylight hours in a
            wheel chair.  His mind is clear and he still has the same keen sense
            of humor that he always had.

                                                                         72


Letter from Carl Fox, Mobile, Alabama, to Mrs. Dot Fox Mallory,
Calhoun City, Miss.  (Continued)


          Hally had a stroke a number of years ago and for several years
has been in the Veteran's Home at Biloxi.  He cannot walk but gets about
in a wheel chair.  He told me not long ago that the Superintendent of
the Home is from Calhoun County, but I have forgotten his name.

          Bessie is also wheel-chair-bound.  She fell and broke her hip
several years ago.

          Z. F. Shelton of Montgomery, Alabama, is a great grandson of
Uncle Jake Fox, one of our grandfather's brothers, whom you probably
remember.  Uncle Jake's daughter Julia Ann married John Gregg, and
their daughter Dora married        Shelton.  Z. F. is their son.  Z. F.
writes that his mother Dora Gregg is 88 years of age, lively, and in
good health.

          Although my family moved to West Point when I was about 12
years of age, I have a very vivid recollection our kin people and many
who were not kin around Slate Springs, particularly all of your family,
Uncle Joe and Aunt Ann, Cusin Sam Fox and Aunt Babe's family, the
Pryors and Spencers, the Mallory's, and others.  As you know, of
course, Aunt Ann and Aunt Babe were my mother's sisters.

          When I was a small boy I heard a story about Uncle Johnny
Fox that is still as funny as the first time I heard it, and it has
stood me in good stead many times when I could not recall someone's
name whom I knew well.  The census taker came around and found Uncle
Johnny on his front porch.  Aunt Louisa was back in the house somewhere.
After giving his own name, John J. Fox, he was asked his wife's first
name.  He had called her Ma since their first child was born.  He thought
for a minute, scratched his head, and turned and hollered, "Oh Ma, what
is your name?"

          My father's and mother's families were all healthy, and I do
not recall many visits from Uncle Tom [father of Dot Fox Mallory].  I
do remember once that I had a pretty bad case of chills and fever, and
after a hard chill and high fever Uncle Tom came, and I was greatly
interested in seeing him roll out a "blue mass" on the bottom of an up-
turned dinner plate, cutting the roll into a number of pieces and rolling
each piece into a pill, and then rolling the pills in flour mixed, as I
recall, with a little soda, and then makinga paste of quinine and water,
or some liquid, and rolling bits of the paste into pills.  Calomel
tablets and capsules came later.

          There have been many births and deaths since my sister Bessie
made the enclosed compilation of the Foxes and their kin.  She did not
have all the available information about your father's and mother's
familv when she made it.  See paqe 14.  I shall aPPreciate it if you will

          complete it and bring it down to date and let me have a copy.  When I

         Exhibit 6                                                                74



           PREFACE BY CARL FOX, SR., OF MOBILE, ALABAMA, DATED DEC. 2, 1954
                TO GENEALOGY COMPILED BY HIS SISTER, BESSIE FOX TRULY


                    Genealogy of One Part of the Fox Family
                    Originating in England and Coming to
                    Virginia, thence through South Carolina,
                    Tennessee, and Alabama to Mississippi,
                    and on to Other States.


                   Preface by Carl Fox, Sr., of Mobile, Alabama


                   The attached genealogy of the Fox line was compiled by my
         sister, Mrs. Bessie Fox Truly, of Fayette, Mississippi, many years ago.
         The personal pronouns "I", "me" and "my" at various places are hers.
         That part of her compilation derived from old family bible records and
         other unqestionable sources available to her among our own close kin-
         folk goes back with certainty to one of two brothers, William and Henry
         Fox, who came from England to Virginia at some time in the 1600's.  One
         of them had a son or grandson named Henry.  This Henry had a son William
         Fox, born in Virginia in or about 1730, who moved to the Richland Dis-
         trict in the fork of the Wateree and Congaree Rivers in South Carolina
         at some time prior to 1768.  The date of his death is not known, but he
         lived to be an old man.  William was the father of Henry Fox, born
         June 12, 1768, died in 1852, whose wife was Sarah Harrell, born July 30,
         1772, died                .  Henry and Sarah were born in South Carolina,
         but lived in their latter days in what is now Webster County, formerly
         a part of old Choctaw County, about two miles south of Slate Springs,
         Mississippi, and died there.  My sister Bessie's compilation traces
         their numerous descendants as far as could be ascertained down to about
         1924, or 1925.

                   There have been so many Foxes of the same name in the successive
         generations that confusion can be avoided only by close attention to the
         dates of their births and deaths.

                   The following is in part a repetition of my sister's compila-
         tion, but somewhat differently arranged.  I have been particularly inter-
         ested in the complex kinship between my father's children and the children
         of his first cousin Malachi Fox, one of the central Mississippi branch.

                   The pages of this preface are numbered A-l, A-2, and so on to
         distinguish them from the paging of my sister's compilation to which I
         refer several times.

                   Henry Fox (1768-1852) and his wife Sarah Harrell (1772-    )
         had seven sons and 5ix daughters.  Among the sons were William, their
         first child, and Thomas, my grandfather, their third.  See page 3, a
         opy of the record in their old family bible which. in 1911. was in the





                                                                               -

-


                                                                                  75

        Preface by Carl Fox, Sr. - Dec. 2, 1954 (Continued)


        possession of James Denton at Sabougla, Mississippi. His mother was
        Mary Fox Denton, daughter of Henry and Sarah.  See page 11.

                  William Fox (1791-    ), eldest child of Henry and Sarah,
        married Priscilla Wooten, an-hey were the progenitors of many of that
        branch in Rankin, Kemper, Hinds, and other counties in central Missis-
        sippi.  William and Priscilla had seven chi1dren (see page 10), among
        them a son Malachi, born in 1813, who married Nancy Hughes, daughter of
        Jesse Hughes and his wife, Dorcas Johnson.  (See Hughes family, page 17).

                  Malachi Fox and his wife Nancy Hughes had children (see page 10),
        among them Priscilla, born about 1837, who married J. P. Starr and lived
        at Brandon in Rankin County.  Malachi also had a son James Fox who i5 not
        mentioned at page 10.  He was much younger than his sister Priscilla, and
        he may have been a son of Malachi by a second marriage.  [yes  I met him
        a year or so after I commenced the practice of law at Vicksburg, Missis-
        sippi, in November, 1900.  We did not know just what our kinship was,
        except that we were both of the same Fox blood, and he wrote to his
        sister, Priscilla Fox Starr, for information.  She was then, in 1902,
        about 65 years of age.  Her reply is set out at pages 22 et seq.  As she
        says in her letter, when she was about 18 she spent a year with her
        mother's parents, Jesse Hughes and Dorcas Johnson Hughes, near Slate
        Springs.  That was about 1854-55.  At that time several of the children,
        many grandchildren and great grandchildren of Henry Fox (the first in
        Mississippi) and Sarah were living in that section.  Forty seven years
        later, when she wrote the letter to her brother Jim, she still had a
        clear recollection of what she learned from them, as well as from her
        own grandfather William Fox.

                  Beginning at page 25 there is a letter from one Joseph E.*
        Steadman of Washington, D. C., to my sister Mrs. Truly.  His investiga-
        tion of the records had been quite extensive and it is hardly to be
        questioned that the Foxes of Virginia and South Carolina mentioned by
        him were of the same origin as the Mississippi Foxes.

                  Thomas Fox (my own "Grand Pappy" Fox) born May 8, 1795, died
        December 29, 1876, was the third child of Henry Fox (the first in Missis-
        sippi) and Sarah.  Thomas married Elizabeth Johnson (the widow Brent by
        a previous marriage).  She was a sister of Dorcas Johnson Hughes, wife
        of Jesse Hughes and mother of Nancy Hughes, who was Malachi Fox's wife.
        See page 5 for the children of Thomas Fox and his wife Elizabeth Johnson,
        copied from his family bible now in my possession.

        died October 16, 1896, was the third child of Thomas Fox and his wife
        Elizabeth Johnson (the widow Brent).  Hally and his brothers and sisters
        and Malachi and his brothers and sisters (children, respectively, of the
        brothers Thomas and William) were first cousins Fox.  Elizabeth Johnson
        Fox, Hally's mother, and Dorcas Johnson Hughes, wife of Jesse Hughes, were
        sisters.  Hally Fox (my father) and his brothers and sisters and the

children of Jesse and Dorcas Johnson Hughes were, therefore, first
cousins on the Johnson side.  (For the children of Jesse and Dorcas
Johnson Hughes, see pages 17-18, among them Nancy and Sarah, and there
was another daughter, Grace, mentioned hereinafter.)  And the first

devoted to each other as if all had been full brothers and sisters -
even my mother's children by her first marriage and my father's first
children who were not related by blood.

          Going back to the kinship between the respective children of
the first cousins Malachi Fox and Hally Fox and their wives, the
sisters Nancy and Sarah Hughes:  They were second cousins of the Fox
blood, second cousins of the Johnson blood, and first cousins of the
Hughes blood.  And that was the relation between Malachi's daughter
Priscilla (Mrs. Starr) and my father's first children, some of whom are
mentioned in her letter to her brother Jim Fox.

          Malachi's children, and Hally's children by his second
marriage, were second cousins of the Fox blood, and second cousins of
the Johnson blood.

          Mrs. Priscilla Fox Starr probably attended the Slate Springs
Academy during the year she spent with her grandparents.  I do not know
when the school was established, but by an Act of the Legislature of
Mississippi, approved February 10, 1860, it was provided:  "That it
shall not be lawful for any person to sell any vinous or spirituous
liquors in any quantity except for medicinal or sacramental purposes
within three miles of Slate Springs Academy, in Calhoun county."

          There is an error at page 18 of my sister Bessie's compilation,

it is stated that Grace, who married Thomas Macon, was the mother of
Willie and others.  They had a son, Tom, who died or was killed in the
Civil War when he was hardly more than a boy.  Grace died and Thomas
Macon married Elizabeth Warner, who was the mother of Will, Jim, Walter,
Mattie and Kirby.  I called her Aunt Elizabeth.  Kirby and I were about
the same age, and I was often at their home.  He and Walter died in
early manhood.  [Note by Shirley Faucette:  This "correction" should

                                                                          78



              EXCERPTS FROM THE WORK OF BESSIE FOX TRULY


                             -1 -


          A persistent tradition in several branches of the Fox family,
 unverified by records, but true, I am sure, says that twin brothers,
 William and Henry (Hally) Fox emigrated from England to the Virginia
 Colony.

          A Henry Fox, or Hally as he was called, was living in Virginia
 in the 1750's.  He was the owner of considerable property.  How many
 children he had is not known, but he had one son named William who
 moved to South Carolina, probably about 1770, and settled in the Rich-
 land District in the forks of the Wateree and Congaree Rivers.  I have
 reason to believe that this Hally Fox also had a son named Thomas.
 For his descendants see page 15.

                          II Generation.

          William was a soldier in the Revolution under General Marion,
and was in the battles of King's Mountain and Cowpens (this was told me
by my father, who also said that my grandmother Fox's grandfather was
in the Revolution.  His name was Johnson).  William Fox had considerable
property, much of which was destroyed by Tories.  When he could get an
opportunity to go home to see his family he was compelled to lie in the
swamps in daytime for fear of the Tories, and his son Hally(Henry) who
was then 10 or 12 years old, would carry him provisions.  He lived to be
very old, probably 80 years, and in his old age took great delight in
rehearsing to his grandsons incidents of the war; the battles in which
he fought, the insults and cruelties of the Tories, etc.  He always
hated the Tories.  One story particularly he would often repeat, how his
wife rebuked the insolence of a British soldier.  "A squad of the
British passed the house while she was churning, and one of them demanded
of her the cream from the churn.  She refused but offered him as much
buttermilk as he wanted.  He stepped in, swearing he would have the
cream; but she, being a very strong woman and also a very resolute one,
caught him up and dashed him out into the yard.  The joke was such a good
one, that the officer in charge abused him for cowardice and made him
leave without any milk."  (The above is an extract from a letter to me
from Mrs. Emma Fox Puthuff, [her half-sister] but I have heard my father
tell this story many, many times.)

          William Fox had a son Henry, born June 12, 1768.

                             III Generation

          Henry Fox, b. June 12, 1768, died 1852, married Sarah Harrell,
b. luly 30, 1772. He settled on, or near, his father's place in South
Carolina, and remained there until 1814 when he removed to Cocke Co.
East Tennessee.  It was a very rough country, and after seeing a fight

                                                             79


Excerpts from the Work of Bessie Fox Truly (Continued)


between two women at a public gathering he moved to Tuscaloosa County,
Alabama, and settled on Sypsy River, 13 miles north of Tuscaloosa -
probably about 1819.  Possibly all of their children, except Dorcas,
were born in South Carolina.

         Henry Fox spent the last years of his life at his home in
Mississippi about two miles south of Slate Springs, Calhoun County,
near "Hally Hall" - my father's home.

         He was very well-to-do - owned many slaves - but cared too
little for money and property to take care of it and develop it.  He
had 13 children, 7 sons and 6 daughters.  The following is a copy of
records in his Bible - now in possession of the Denton family of
Calhoun County:  [Mississippi

                           IV Generation

           Thomas Fox, born in South Carolina, May 8, 1795, died
 Dec. 29, 1876, married Feb. 19, 1815 Mrs. Elizabeth Johnson
 (Nov. 18, 1790 - Nov. 9, 1860) Brent, widow.  Extract from letter
 of Mrs. Emma Fox Puthuff, relating what was told her by my father:

           "During the journey from South Carolina to Tennessee,
     about 1814, one of the teams took  fright and ran away.  The
     wagon was overturned and Elizabeth Brent and her child were
     thrown out.  Elizabeth's arm was broken and the boy's upper
     lip was cut open.  Thomas Fox then drove her the remainder of
     the way in the traveling chair in which his father and mother
     had been journeying.  Soon after settling in Tennessee these
     two were married, Feb. 19, 1815. They had a "big wedding."
     Here also Polly Fox (Mary, sister to Thomas) was married to
     David Denton of Tennessee, and perhaps their first child was
     born here, whom they called Joseph.  Cousin Martha Cooke was
     their daughter (see p. B  ).  Also Sarah Hughes (my father's
     first wife) was born in Tennessee."


           Mrs. Elizabeth Brent had two children when my grandfather
 married her - or rather, from the records, I think she had four
 children, only one of whom, William, was living (see below).  She was
 full Irish - her mother was born in Ireland.*

           Thomas Fox and his wife removed to Tuscaloosa County,
 Alabama, 1819, where he remained 15 years, then came to Mississippi
 to "Choctaw Co. and settled a farm on Sabougla Creek."  They had 8
 children, and I think all were born in Alabama.



                       [end of her page -4-]

 her father was Benjamin Johnson, whose mother probably was Mary
 Claiborne Fox, daughter of William Fox and his wife Sarah Avent Fox,
 as shown in Section I of this collection.  Shirley Faucette]
