          "Dr. Thomas J. Fox, druggist and physician, Slate Springs, Miss.
The father of Dr. Fox, Thomas Fox, was a native of South Carolina, born
in 1795, and when a young man moved with his parents to Tennessee, where
he [Thomas Sr.] was married to Mrs. Elizabeth [Johnson] Brent.  She was
born in 1790 and was of Irish descent, her mother having been born in the
Emerald isle [Ann (Nancy) Johnson, wife of Benjamin Johnson].  She was a
number of years her husband's senior.  Their marriage resulted in the
birth of eight children, who are named in the order of their birth, as
follows:  Unity (deceased), John farmer at Slate Springs), Henry (at
West Point), Mrs. Sarah Lay (deceased), Joseph (merchant, married, and
resides at Slate Springs), Jesse H. (died in Texas), Dr. Thomas J.
(subject), and David D. deceased).  After his marriage Thomas [Sr.]
moved to Alabama, and remained there about fifteen years, and then
located on a place near Slate Springs, in Choctaw county, Miss....

          "His [Thomas Sr.'s] father, Henry Fox, was a native of South
Carolina and a descendant of a representative family of that state.
Late in life the latter moved to Mississippi, settled near Slate Springs,
and there died at the age of eighty-four years.  He [Henry] was a member
of the Baptist Church, to which he was a liberal contributor, and in
politics a stanch Whig.  His family consisted of eight children, most of
whom lived to be grown, married and have families." [Henry Fox had
thirteen children; Thomas Fox, Sr., had eight children.]



Exhibit 8                                                                82



  EXCERPTS FROM

TRAILING THE FOXES

                       ,

                             by James C. Fox


[The following excerpts are from narratives contained in a booklet
"Trailing the Foxes" written by James C. Fox, of Monticello, Arkansas,
and given to Shirley Faucette by James C. Fox a few years prior to
his death.  "Cousin Jimmie" was one of the organizers of the "Fox
Family Reunion" which began annual meetings in 1955, and he served
as the second president of that group.  He was intensely interested
in Fox history and is responsible for the gathering of information
on a large number of the descendants of Henry and Sarah Harrell Fox.
He was a descendant of Henry's fourth child, Henry Fox, Jr., and his
wife Susan Doughty.]


          This brings us to Grandpappy Henry Fox, 1768-1852, who is the
ancestor of most all the Fox line in Miss., and whose descendants, true
to their tradition, have ever moved on to new frontiers.  They are
especially numerous in Miss., Arkansas and a host of them in Texas as well
as many other states west and north.  There are many, many related fami-
lies in Calhoun and Webster Counties, as well as some central counties of
Mississippi and they are to be found in all walks of life.

          What folk-lore the writer has been able to obtain will now be
carried along with the genealogy of the line and nothing is entered that
isn't considered authentic, as what folk-lore is given has come down
through different lines of the family.  The first years of Grandpappy's
married life seem rather stringent but that was only a few years after
the Revolution and could be in part because the needed things for house-
keeping could not be procured rather than not having the money to buy.

          Grandpappy Henry Fox was born in Richland District, S.C. 1768,
where he married and most of this thirteen children were born, later
moving to East Tennessee, where it is told that after seeing two women
have a fight at some public gathering, he decided to make another move,
going then to Tuscaloosa Co., Ala., where perhaps his last two children
were born.  In 1835 he moved with several children and grandchildren to
Choctaw Miss., now being Webster Co., Webster Co. being taken from a
portion of Choctaw.  [Webster County was formed in 1874 and the name
changed to Sumner, but changed to Webster in 1882.]

          The tradition that comes to the writer...is that he came to be
called "grandpappy Henry" late in his life, being as he was, sire of all
the Fox Family of and near Slate Springs, Miss.,...with wife, four sons,
two daughters, their families and perhaps several families of friends as
they were among the very first settlers in that part of the state.

                                                                          83
Trailing the Foxes - by James C. Fox (Continued)


Another son, Jacob Fox, with family and friends came to this locality
after the Civil War.  Grandpappy's first son, William, moved from Ala.
to somewhere east of Jackson, Miss.  (Brandon, Miss.) and became the
father of many descendants in that part of Miss.  [William Fox went to
DeKalb, Miss., Kemper County, and William's son Malachi later moved to
Brandon, Miss., Rankin County.   Another son, Eliab Fox, moved to
Forest, Miss.]  His son, John, moved to Alabama, somewhere east of
Mobile and lived out his life there and said to have become wealthy.
Records available give him as having only one child, a daughter.

          Folk-lore of grandpappy Henry Fox, as coming down through the
family of his daughter Unity Fox Pryor and the daughter of Unity Fox
Pryor, Martha Francis Pryor Golding, was told to the writer many years
ago by the husband of Martha Francis Pryor, John R. Golding, who was a
Civil War Veteran and is buried i Lone Sassafras Cemetery in Drew Co.,
Arkansas.  Tis said that when Grandpappy Henry and his wife Sarah began
keeping house, their bed was a cowhide and a quilt laid on the floor,
later building a bed with one end attached to the wall and legs under
the other end.  Tis said that they did, thier milking in some very large
gourds, also their churning in like manner.  Their table forks were
made from cane that grew in abundance nearby.  At the noon hour, during
the tilling of his crops, he would sometimes hobble his horse out to
graze while he partook of his noon meal and at times would do a bit of
fishing in the stream at his place before returning to the field.

          Quite a few years before grandpappy answered the final summons,
by his untiring efforts he had accumulated all of this worlds goods that
he wished, expressing complete satisfaction in the fulness of his life
and not wishing any more material wealth.  He turned over most of his
duties to his chief negro slave who also acted much as his personal
valet, laying out what suits he wished his master to wear when dressing
for any occasion.

          Grandpappy was a liberal church supporter and in politics was
a staunch Whig.  Tis said of Grandpappy that he was very kind to his
slaves and that in the marrying of slaves with those of the neighbors, he
would either buy or sell with them, whichever was the most suitable, so
that the slaves might live together, but that he never sold a slave
against his will.  Slaves were allowed to unite with the first Baptist
church of the community.  The writer has personally examined the original
records which carried the names of several colored members....

          Grandpappy Henry, his children and grandchildren and their wives
were prominent in organizing the first Baptist Church of the community,
the Bethany Baptist Church, now located in Slate Springs, Calhoun Co.  It
has been a continuous organization since its establishment July 16, 1836.
Grandpappy Henry and one of his sons, Thomas Fox, were among the first
deacons of this church.  For many years the family were very prominent
in the affairs of this church.  Today, though there are many of the
related families who make up a large part of the membership in this
hllrrh_ thr re nnne nf t.he Fnx nme.

                                                                         l

                                                                         84
Trailina the Foxes - by James C. Fox (Continued)


         Grandpappy Henry was sixty-seven years old when he made his
last move to take up life anew in the virgin forest, in the rolling hills
of Mississippi, selecting a tract of land with a nice running spring, and
building his house on a slight knoll, overlooking the spring.  There is
nothing today remaining to remind one of this house site, except one huge
white oak tree, that was evidently topped many years ago as its height is
small in proportion to its great size.  The spring, tho neglected, is
still running a nice stream of clear water and is used for stock.

         Grandpappy Henry and wife, Sarah, had been resting in unmarked
graves for one hundred and two years without a stone, but through the
kindly aid of their great granddaughter, Mrs. Dot Mallory, of Calhoun
City, Miss., and who is the oldest of the clan in Calhoun, and also by a
letter written by Priscilla Fox Starr in about 1905 (who was a great
granddaughter of grandpappy Henry) we have been able to positively iden-
tify their graves, which is near that of their daughter Mary Fox Denton
and her husband, David Denton, which have a stone marker.  The graves of
this illustrious pair are now marked with a nice flat stone, placed
there by Verner Mallory and the writer.  The cost of this stone was
shared equally by Mrs. Dot Mallory (Mrs. Verner Mallory), Carl Fox, Sr.,
now of Mobile, Ala., who is another one of grandpappy's great grandsons,
and the writer.  These graves are in a pasture now owned by Mr. Vardaman
Springer, about two miles southwest of Slate Springs, Miss., in Calhoun
County, but this cemetery is just south of the county line in Webster Co.



[Note by Shirley Faucette:  In 1960 the descendants of Henry Fox and his
wife, Sarah Harrell Fox, as a project of the annual Family Reunions,
erected a historical marker in his cemetery.  For the text of the marker
see Exhibit 14 of this section.l


                          * * *



         "Trailing the Fox line through Henry Fox, Jr., born 3-17-1798,
in S.C.  We have less on this fourth son of Grandpappy Henry than any of
his seven sons.  This Henry Jr., who is the great grandfather of the
writer [James C. Fox] would have been seventeen years old at the time
they moved to Alabama.  It is known that he married a Miss Susan Doughty
and had two girls and two boys when the family moved to Choctaw Co., now
Webster, and settled with several other members of the family near the
present town of Slate Springs, this town being just across the county
line in which they had settled.

         This Henry Fox, Jr., was one of the charter members of Bethany
Baptist Church at or near Slate Springs.  According to a letter written
by Priscilla Virginia Fox Starr in 1902, and relating events while she
was visitinq at Slate Springs in 1855, this Henry Fox, Jr., was still

                                                                          85
Trailin the Foxes - by James C. Fox (Continued)


living there at that time.  There is no accompanying name for his wife
in the Church records as in the case of others of the family and the
writer has found no grave with his name.  The writer's Grandfather,
Jacob Fox, had to be one of the two boys of this Henry Fox, Jr., as
they moved from Ala., as this Jacob Fox was born about 1829-30.  Jacob
Fox, nephew of the older Jacob, died in a Confederate army camp about
the year 1863-64.  No word or records has been found by this writer
as to what happened to tnis Henry Fox, Jr., and the other members of
this line of the family.  There are a number of unmarked graves at the
place where Grandpappy Henry and wife, Sarah, and Mary Fox Denton are
buried.  Some once, had shelters over them but seems that they have
been down many years.  Some just had "pailings" built around them, a
small wooden fence, but they, too, have been down many years.  It is
only a conjecture that some of these are this Henry Fox, Jr., and his
children.

          My most hearty thanks goes to Z. F. Shelton of Montgomery, Ala.
and another great great grandson of Grandpappy Henry Fox for the informa-
tion that Henry Fox, Jr., had the two girls and two boys when he migrated
from Alabama with others of the family to Choctaw Co., Miss....  My
father, David Henry Fox, was about ten years old when Grandfather Jacob
died in an Army Camp, my father being the oldest of the four children,
three boys and one girl.  Three of these, Father, one uncle and my aunt
migrated to Drew Co., Ark., with their families when the writer was at
the tender age of five and third child of the family.  Sixty years
later the writer returned to the place of his birth to meet the children
of Uncle Jerry Fox, who remained near the old home grounds and raised a
very large family.  It was on reading a History of the Bethany Baptist
Church, now of Slate Springs, organized in 1836, with many names of the
Fox Family, that the writer became interested in family history.  That
has led inquiries to individuals of a number of states, as well as data
secured from State Archives from three states and Washington.

          My father, David Fox (1853-1909) was the oldest of four
children in his family and had to share with his mother the care of the
other three and the family andwas thereby prevented from receiving any
schooling....  My Father became a good penman, a good reader, and true
to the Fox tradition was a Missionary Baptist and an ardent student of
the Bible.  He served as a Justice of the Peace for several years in
Arkansas and was highly respected by both the undesirable as well as the
desirable elements of society.  He fell dead while plowing, and his last
step was of normal length.

          Joseph C. Fox (1-25-1802 to 6-30-1879) and his wife Margaret
Crossland (5-19-1808 to 5-30-1876).  (Her birth date shown elsewhere as
1805)  Married in Alabama.  They are buried in old Bethany Cemetery near
Slate Springs, Miss.  He migrated to Choctaw County with his family in
1835.  He was among the charter members of the Bethany Baptist Church.
He served as the first postmaster of Slate Springs, Miss.  There is an
old two-story log house on the property once owned by Joseph Fox, now
owned by a late descendant of the family, Lon Pryor, Jr., who has negro
tenants in the house.  As told by the oldest citizens of the community,
it was built by Joseph Fox.  The logs were hewn from very large trees,
hewn on four sides some six or eight inches in thickness and about
fourteen inches in depth, locked at the corners with dove-tailing.


                          * * *



          Trailing the Foxes through the line of James Thigpen Fox
(7-30-1814 to 9-22-1883).  He was a charter member of Bethany Baptist
Church and was ordained into the ministry in that church and served
them as their pastor for 30 years.  He received 431 members into the
church during his ministry.  Rev. Jimmie Fox was loved and revered by
his people as few ministers are.  Today the later generations of this
locality speak his name with reverence.  Rev. Jimmie Fox, after his
father's death, is reported to have lived in his father's log house
for a time, and the last white family to occupy it.  He went to Texas
and lived there for a time but came back to Calhoun Co., where he died
and is buried in the Bethany cemetery.  Rev. Jimmie's wife went back
to Texas after his death to live with her sister, somewhere around
Sherman, Texas.  She is buried out there.



                          * * 

                                                                          87
Trailing the Foxes - by James C. Fox (Continued)


          Dr. Thomas J. Fox raised his family in the southwest outskirts
of Slate Springs and bought the property from Thomas Perry Macon.  He
owned considerable land.  As told by his daughter, Mrs. Dot Mallory,
Dr. Fox twice offered his service to the Confederate Army and twice was
told to go back to the women and children of his community, who needed
him more than the army.  Also told by Mrs. Mallory of a raid the Union
troops made on his farm, taking all of his horses but one and later
returning for his last horse.  The doctor pleaded and begged in vain to
leave him his one remaining horse so that he might be able to serve his
community as their comforting docor, but as the Union troops were lead-
ing his horse away, being a Mason, he gave the distress sign to the man
in charge of the troops, whereupon the horse was promptly led back to
his quarters.

          Dr. Thomas Fox was loved and revered second only to the ministers
of the Gospel.

                                                                         88
Trailing the Foxes - by James C. Fox (Continued)


          History of Bethany Baptist Church at Slate Springs, Missis-
sippi, written on the Centennial of the Church, on Aug. 6, 1936, by
Mrs. Jimmie Phillips of Slate Springs.  [Mrs. Jimmie Vance Phillips
was a descendant of Joseph C . Fox, son of Henry Fox and Sarah Harrel 1
FrY 1


          Bethany Baptist Church was established in Straughn's house,
supposed to be about one half mile west of present site of church, on
July 16, 1836.  The first building was erected two miles southwest of Slate
Springs where Bethany Cemetery now is.  The first building was of logs
with the board roof and the door facings fastened with wood pins.  The
seats were similarly constructed.

          The next building was of sawed lumber, built near the first
one and the old building was sold for 6.00.  The church was moved
into the present building in 1881.  The first pastor was Elder James
Martin and the first deacons were Henry Fox Sr., Thomas Fox Sr. and
David Denton.  The first clerk was Thomas Fox Sr.  The church has had
19 pastors, as follows....

          ...Rev. Jimmie Fox served as pastor for thirty years and
received into the church 431 members....  The records of the first
thirty-five years have been preserved and for the later period from
1918.  [Minutes of this church are on file in the Archives of the
Mississippi Baptist Historical Commission, Clinton, Mississippi.]

          The names of the members who were examined on the twelve
articles of faith and appear in the constitution were:  Oliver P.
Martin, Henry Fox Sr, Joseph C. Fox, Thomas Fox, John Fox, Henry Fox
Jr. David Denton, William C. Denmon, Travis Straughn, Polly Martin,
Unity Martin, Sarah Fox, Margarett Fox, Elizabeth Fox, Mary Denton,
Dorcas Johnson, Ann Easley, Celia Long, Dorcas G. Denmon and Ann
Crossland; Guy - Peter - Amy, Colored.  Presbytery; Medey White, James
Martin, Eli Langford; no descendants of the Martins now live in this
vicinity; Henry Fox Sr. was the father of the four sons - Thomas,
Joseph C., Henry Jr, and Rev. Jimmie Fox, two daughters Mary Denton
and Unity Pryor.

          Exhibit 9                                                                89



                           NOTES ON THE JACOB FOX FAMILY

                              by Z. F. Shelton, 1956


                    Jacob Fox was about six years old when his family moved from
          Richland County, South Carolina, in 1814 to Cocke County, Tennessee.
          The family again moved in 1819 and settled on the Sipsey River above
          Tuscaloosa in Tuscaloosa County, Alabama.

                    Jacob Fox was born in Richland County, South Carolina,
          January 19, 1808, and married in Tuscaloosa County, Ala., March 26, 1829,
          to Mary Ann Caroline (Polly) Hughes-Finnell, widow of Leroy M. Finnell,
          who was killed at Northport, Ala., December 12, 1827, while trying to
          separate two men who were fighting.  Mary A. C. was a daughter of Jesse
          Hughes and his wife Dorcas Johnson, who was a sister to the widow Eliza-
          beth Johnson-Brent, who married Thomas Fox, older brother of Jacob.

                    Jacob and Mary A. C. Fox had eight children, five of whom were
          born in Tuscaloosa County, then in 1841 this family moved to the Palmetto
          community in Pickens County, Ala., where the last three were born.  Of
          their eight children, seven were girls and their only son was John M.,
          who married and had only a daughter, Rosalie, who married a Daniels.
          With the death of John M. Fox went the Fox name in the Jacob Fox line.

                    One humorous incident that happened to Jacob soon after he moved
          to the Palmetto community is remembered by all the old families.  Jacob
          was going along the road near Unity Grove Methodist Church when he met
          another settler and introduced himself with:  "My name is Jacob Fox";  the
          other replied "My name is Jacob Coons" and they almost had a fight, each
          thinking the other was kidding him.

                    There are many valuable records on the Fox families in Tuscaloosa
          County but all records in Pickens County burned in 1876 and only Federal
          land grants and census records are available.  Jacob and Mary A. C. Fox
          lived in Pickens 25 years and all their children married there except the
          youngest,Angeline, who married after the family moved to Mississippi.
          Dorcas Bennett-Hollingsworth was the only child of Jacob who stayed in
          Pickens County and she is buried at Unity Grove.  After the Civil War, and
          in the fall of 1866, Jacob Fox and all his children (except Dorcas) and
          their families moved to Choctaw (now Webster) County, Miss., and settled
          in the community between Bellefontaine and Slate Springs where his father
          Henry and most of his brothers and sisters had settled in the 1830's.
          Jesse and Dorcas Hughes, parents of Mary A.C., had settled in Bellefontaine.

                    When the families moved to Mississippi, Joel Dorroh later known
          as Rev. Joel Dorroh), a grandson of Jacob and Mary A. C. Fox, was a lad
          only eight years of age, but drove a yoke of oxen in the "wagon train" all
          the way from Alabama to Mississippi because of the shortage of men in the
          Jacob Fox family.  Mary A. C. Hughes had only one son by her first marriage
          to Leroy M. Finnell, and this son, Leroy Jr., died in Pickens County, Ala.,

                  NOTES ON THE JACOB FOX FAMILY

                     by Z. F. Shelton, 1956


          Jacob Fox was about six years old when his family moved from
Richland County, South Carolina, in 1814 to Cocke County, Tennessee.
The family again moved in 1819 and settled on the Sipsey River above
Tuscaloosa in Tuscaloosa County, Alabama.

          Jacob Fox was born in Richland County, South Carolina,
January 19, 1808, and married in Tuscaloosa County, Ala., March 26, 1829,
to Mary Ann Caroline (Polly) Hughes-Finnell, widow of Leroy M. Finnell,
who was killed at Northport, Ala., December 12, 1827, while trying to
separate two men who were fighting.  Mary A. C. was a daughter of Jesse
Hughes and his wife Dorcas Johnson, who was a sister to the widow Eliza-
beth Johnson-Brent, who married Thomas Fox, older brother of Jacob.

          Jacob and Mary A. C. Fox had eight children, five of whom were
born in Tuscaloosa County, then in 1841 this family moved to the Palmetto
community in Pickens County, Ala., where the last three were born.  Of
their eight children, seven were girls and their only son was John M.,
who married and had only a daughter, Rosalie, who married a Daniels.
With the death of John M. Fox went the Fox name in the Jacob Fox line.

          One humorous incident that happened to Jacob soon after he moved
to the Palmetto community is remembered by all the old families.  Jacob
was going along the road near Unity Grove Methodist Church when he met
another settler and introduced himself with:  "My name is Jacob Fox";  the
other replied "My name is Jacob Coons" and they almost had a fight, each
thinking the other was kidding him.

          There are many valuable records on the Fox families in Tuscaloosa
County but all records in Pickens County burned in 1876 and only Federal
land grants and census records are available.  Jacob and Mary A. C. Fox
lived in Pickens 25 years and all their children married there except the
youngest,Angeline, who married after the family moved to Mississippi.
Dorcas Bennett-Hollingsworth was the only child of Jacob who stayed in
Pickens County and she is buried at Unity Grove.  After the Civil War, and
in the fall of 1866, Jacob Fox and all his children (except Dorcas) and
their families moved to Choctaw (now Webster County, lliss., and settled
in the community between Bellefontaine and Slate Springs where his father
Henry and most of his brothers and sisters had settled in the 1830's.
Jesse and Dorcas Hughes, parents of Mary A.C., had settled in Bellefontaine.

          When the families moved to Mississippi, Joel Dorroh (later known
as Rev. Joel Dorroh), a grandson of Jacob and Mary A. C. Fox, was a lad
only eight years of age, but drove a yoke of oxen in the "wagon train" all
the way from Alabama to Mississippi because of the shortage of men in the
Jacob Fox family.  Mary A. C. Hughes had only one son by her first marriage
to Leroy M. Finnell. and this son, Leroy Jr.. died in Pickens County, Ala.,

                                                             9o



Notes on the Jacob Fox Family - by Z. F. Shelton (Continued)


in 1867, leaving his second wife, Lucinda Cook and their three sma11
children:  "Mag", Jesse and John Finnell.  Dora Gregg-Shelton stated
before she died in 1955 that "soon after Leroy Jr. died my grandfather,
Jacob Fox, went to Alabama and brought "Mag", Jesse, and John to his
home in Miss."  Joel Dorroh later married the girl "Mag".

          In the 1870's, some years before Jacob died, his daughters
Mary Prewitt, Martha Gregg, and Angeline Hicks, his son John M., and
all their families moved to Valley Mills, Texas.  This left three
daughters, Sarah Dorroh, Julia Ann Gregg, and Jerusha White living near
him when he died in 1884, Dec. 8.  Mary A. C. died Oct. 25, 1896, then
in 1903 Jerusha and her husband Henry White (son of Rebecca Shelton-
White) moved to Hartshorne, Oklahoma.

          These families were very religious, tenderhearted, and
sympathetic despite the hardness of pioneer life and have left their
marks of rigid morality on the communities where they lived and on
the descendant families.

          .



                     NOTES ON MALACHI FOX

      AS TOLD BY HIS SON, JOHN HENRY FOX, TO JOHN HENRY FOX, JR.


          Malachi Fox was Mayor of DeKalb, Miss. Papa says he was mayor
and Justice of the Peace everywhere he went. He was also first mayor of
Tuscaloosa, Ala., and it was he who had all the large water oaks set out
on the main streets of Tuscaloosa which are so large now. [Note by Emma
Shirley Fox Faucette, who copied these notes from her brother, John H.
Fox, Jr., says: Cousin Katie Fox Runge says it was William Fox, father
of Malachi, who was mayor of Tuscaloosa. This is believed correct, as
Malachi was born 2-10-1813, and would have been 20 years or younger when
he moved from Alabama to Mississippi, whereas his father William was
born 3-6-1791 and would have been in his early 40's when he left Alabama.]

          When he moved to DeKalb there had been a great deal of trouble
between the town people and some very rough people who lived in the
county round about, and the town people told Mala about the trouble when
he arrived. He told them that if they would stick by him they could put
a stop to all the fighting and hell-raising this rough element was con-     j
ducting. So they elected him mayor and he got his band together and         3
fought it out hand to hand with this element until finally there were
only two or three of them left. One afternoon Mala was alone in his
office and they told him they were tired of his bullying and they were
going now to get him once and for all. One of them began to sidle
around behind him, and just as he got in front of a window that was
pretty high off the floor, Mala made a lunge at him and toppled him out
the window, leaving only two to confront him. There happened to be a
hatchet on a stove in the room and Mala dived for it and brandished it
at the remaining two who fled incontinently.

          On another occasion, Mala had a man down in the street with a
strangle hold on his throat and was choking him so badly that finally
the man drew a knife from his pocket and slit it across Mala's wrist and
cut him so badly that all the nerves were severed and that hand was so
dead for forty years that he could knock a door down with that hand and
never feel it although his fist would be bloodied. He would even go to
sleep at nights smoking his pipe by his tobacco table and let the hand
fall against his table lamp chimney and badly burn his hand without
knowing it until someone awakened him.

         Mala told Papa [John Henry Fox] that the first cotton gin he
ever saw resulted from the fact that Mala's father William Fox went off
somewhere when Mala was a small boy and saw a gin and came home and made
himself a gin. Mala was so small he had to stand on a box to work the
bellows while his father William heated the steel and beat out the saws
and gin head for this gin. William's gin was the first in that part of
the country and they stacked everybody's cotton in piles in a wareroom
on the second floor and as it was ginned stamped it through a square hole
in the floor into a big bag hanging to the ceiling of the first floor.

                                                                          92



Notes on Malachi Fox (Continued)


          One day Mala and a few of his friends got into the gin where
everybody's cotton was piled separately and in playing levelled and
mixed everybody's cotton together.  The next day William pulled down a
piece of leather belting and told Mala he would have to whip him for
that.  Mala told him he thought he had grown too big to whip and didn't
believe he'd take it but would run away if he was whipped.  William
just hung the strap back over a ratter and never whipped him again, but
told him if he was big enough not to be whipped he was big enough not
to be destructive and thoughtless.

          Mala said that when the rough country element had all been
run away except one man who persisted in giving trouble, they made it up
to get rid of him.  A quarrel was picked with him by one of the townsmen
and finally it got so bad a duel was arranged.  Mala gave the townsman a
shotgun loaded with blank shells and the country badman a shotgun loaded
with red ripe polk berries.  Both shot, and the townsman's white shirt
streamed with the blood-red polkberry juice.  He fell to the ground
feigning death and kicking his last.  The others then told the country
man that he had killed the fellow and he had better skip.  He broke
away through the swamp and was never heard from or seen again.









    [The above account was written down by John Henry Fox, Jr., as
    his father, John Henry Fox, son of Malachi Fox, related these
    happenings.  These notes are from a copy made by Emma Shirley
    Fox Faucette dauahter of John Henry Fox.l

                           EXTRACTS FROM

                       NOTES ON MALACHI FOX

AS TOLD BY HIS SON WILL FOX, TO HIS DAUGHTER ELEANOR FOX PENNINGTON



          Malachi Fox was born in South Carolina on February 10, 1813,
the son of William and Priscilla Wooten Fox.  He moved from South
Carolina with his parents, brothers and sisters, and other families
who were kin to them.  His parents stopped in Alabama for a while
but the rest went on to North Mississippi and settled around Slate
Springs.  Just where they settled in Alabama I do not know, perhaps
around Selma or Evergreen.  At one time Mala Fox took a contract to
set out evergreens in a town that is now known as Evergreen, Alabama.
He married Nancy Ann Hughs of Alabama and it may be that he and his
father came on to Mississippi together for both families went to
Kemper County, Mississippi, but one or the other may have gone there
first.

                         * * *


          Mala's first wife died and is buried at Cross Roads near
DeKalb, Miss.  Afterwards he married Martha Ellen Skinner who was born
in Greene County, Alabama, but who was then living with her people at
DeKalb, Miss.  Her mother's people were Jones and were Welsh.

                        * * *


          Grandpa Skinner was born in 1804.  After the marriage of his
daughter to Mala Fox, he moved next to their home at Fannin when he was
old.  Grandma Skinner lived with Dad's mother and father after his death.
She fell over a log and went on crutches afterwards.  She died at Eliza's
home Eliza Sanderson, sister of Martha Ellen Skinner] when Dad was
about twelve, (1882).

                       I  


          Soon after Mala and Ellen Fox were married they moved to Marion,
Miss., [Note was inserted by Eleanor Fox Ponder here which said"'Rose Hill'
was their place". and Aunt Mary may have been born there.  They lived at
several other places, finally going to Brandon where he had a general
store.  The Yankees burned him out and he moved to Fannin on a farm where
the rest of the children from Aunt Nannie on down, were born.  When Daddv
was about 10 (1880) he moved back to Brandon, Miss., where Dad went to
the old male Academy and to a school taught by a Mr. Myers. (?)

          Daddy's father had the first cotton gin in North Mississippi and
the first one in Alabama.  Mala often told his children about having to
work the bellows at the blacksmith shop so his father, William Fox, could
make some of the machinery for the gin in Alabama.  [This was not Will
Fox's father, as indicated, but his grandfather, William Fox, as is evident

                                                                         94




Notes on Malachi Fox (Continued)



          Mala Fox, esquire, was the eldest son of William [Fox] and
Virginia Priscilla Wooten.  He was born in South Carolina on Feb. 10,
1813.  When his family arrived in Kemper County he was four years old.*
He often said he was four years older than his adopted state of Missis-
sippi.  His only recollection of his native South Carolina home was
going to the split rail fence where the cow was milked, and finding
the calf rope tied to the fence, he decided to play the part of the
calf and had slipped the noose end of the rope over his head, when an
angry bull took after him.  He fell in trying to get away from the
bellowing bull, and when his father got to him he was choked uncon-
scious.  His father, thinking it best for Mala to learn by experience,
laughed at him.

          When moving to Mississippi in the covered wagons, they put
the demijon of fine brandy up between the feather beds.  Mala, to
amuse himself, would pull the cork enough to let the brandy drip into
his mouth as it sloshed.  He found it good and there is where he stayed
when he was tired of walking.

























          *[Note by Shirley Faucette:  While it is true that Malachi was
           four years older than his adopted state of Mississippi, it is
           thought that he and his family first migrated to Tennessee and
           then to Alabama before moving to Mississippi in the early
           1830's.  There is a record of Malachi Fox selling land to his
           grandfather, Henry Fox, in Alabama in November 1833.  See
           Paae 63.1

                        EXCERPT FROM

                   THE BRANDON REPUBLICAN

                                  .

                Brandon, Mississippi, 1886



ANOTHER GOOD CITIZEN GONE


Again we are called upon to chronicle the death of one of our oldest
and best citizens, Malachi ("Mala") Fox, Esq., who died at his resi-
dence near Brandon on Tuesday last at eleven o'clock P.M.  He was
about 72 years of age and had been a citizen of Rankin County for
about 35 years.  He was apparently as well as usual a few hours before
his death.  He was suddenly stricken and at once seemed to realize
that the time of his departure was very near at hand, and resigned
himself with fortitude to the will of "Him who doeth all things well."
He died in full fellowship with the Methodist Church, and was, we
believe, a consistent member thereof.  He leaves beside his widow
and children many other relatives, and friends innumerable to mourn
his death.  May the turf rest lightly upon the grave of our friend.


[Born 2-10-1813, died 3-17-1886]       [Ed. Frantz, Editor]






                          Also from

             THE BRANDON REPUBLICAN      [date not known]


         Our old friend, "Mala" (Malachi) Fox, of this county (Rankin)
was appointed Foreman for the Federal Grand Jury at Jackson by Judge
Hill.  Three years ago he was appointed Foreman of the Petit Jury by
the same authority and was highly complimented by the judge and District
Attorney.  He is an honest and conscientious man and knows about as much
law (probably more) as the majority of lawyers.  At one time he was
mayor of DeKalb, Kemper County, Miss.  He was also a public officer,
Brandon, Miss., First Justice of the Peace, and Mayor of Brandon
1859-1863).

 Exhibit 12                                                                96



                       NOTES ON DORCAS FOX, WIFE OF BENAGY L. MILLS


         [The following notes on Dorcas Fox, youngest child of
         Henry and Sarah Harrell Fox, were given to Shirley
         Faucette by Walter S. Shuman, Jr., of Amarillo, Texas.]


           Dorcas Fox was born 21 June 1816.  She was married to Benagy L.
Mills the  9th of February 1832 by Thomas Fox at Tuscaloosa, Alabama.
There she lived until the end of the Civil War.  At the close of the War,
when the slaves were freed, she became frightened and decided to leave
Alabama as many others were doing at that time.  Although her husband was
still in service (believed to be a doctor in the Confederate Army) she
gathered her children together and left for Texas in a covered wagon.
She was later joined by her husband and settled in Denton County, Texas
in Shiloh community.  The 1880 census shows her living with her eldest
son James H. Mills.  She died later that year and is now buried in Shiloh
cemetery located about ten miles west of Lewisville, Texas.



            EXCERPTS FROM THE OBITUARY OF DORCUS MARIE MILLS KNIGHT


           Death claimed Mrs. Dorcus Marie "Grandmother" Knight, 97,
Swisher county's oldest citizen, Friday night, Nov. 5, 1943...in the
Valley View [Texas] community....

           Shortly after her marriage, her husband became a Confederate
soldier in the company known as the "Tuscaloosa Plowboys".  At the close
of the war, she and her husband migrated to Texas in a wagon in 1865.
They settled in Johnson County for seven  years, later homesteading in
Eastland County.  Coming to Swisher County in 1891, they were among the
pioneers of this section of the Plains.  Their first home was a dugout
east of Vigo Park.  Five years later they bought a place in the Salem
community, paying $350.00 for a fenced section which was well improved
for those days.  A fair conditioned three room house with a pantry, porch
and two windmills were included on the new location.  Here she experienced
all that went with a pioneer life on the plains....  Listening to incidents
of her life one clearly sees "Time Marching On" from pre-Civil War days,
through the war, through Indian strife, the Spanish-American War and the
World War, for her life covered almost a century of time.  She saw the
slaves freed and knew first hand, what those re-construction days meant
to the South.  She lived in Texas when it was a young state having its
Indian troubles and told of the fear of the red man.  She lived near Blue
Mound on Indian Creek when the Indians were so depredating there.  She
told of hiding her children under the floor of her home when the Indians
were near.  She knew the "West" when it was full of hardships for the
pioneer....

[Dorcas Marie Mills Knight, wife of Ellis Gore Knight, and daughter of
Benagy and Dorcas Fox Mills.]

                              WILL OF ANN JOHNSON


             (Dated February 3, 1821 - Probated October 15. 1825

I                 In the name of God Amen; I Ann Johnson of the State of Tennessee
and County of Cocke being sick and weak in body but of perfect mind, thanks
be given unto God.  Calling to mind the mortality of my body that it is
appointed for all men once to die do make and ordain this my last will and
testament that is to say principally and first of all I give and recommend
my soul into the hands of almighty God that gave it and my body I commend
to the earth to be buried in a decent Christian burial at the discretion
of the Executors nothing doubting but at the General Resurrection I shall
receive the same again by the mighty power of God; and as touching such
worldly Estate which it has pleased God to bless me in this life I give
demur and dispose of the same in the following manner and form principally
and first of all my just debts and funeral charges to be paid; secondly
I give and bequeath unto my son John C. Johnson my land lying on the
French Broad River and my negro man Jim and all my stock of cattle.  And
I do give unto the children of my daughter Unity, deceased, namely John
Brent, William Brent, James Brent, and Thomas Brent one hundred dollars
each which the said John C. Johnson is hereby compelled to pay to the
above named children in consequence of his receiving the above mentioned
land and negro and cattle, the childrens' money to be paid to them by
the said John C. Johnson when each shall reach the age of twenty one
years and not before.  Thirdly I give and bequeath unto my daughter
Elizabeth Fox my negro boy Peter.  Fourthly I do give and bequeath unto
my daughter Dorcas Hughes Five Hundred Dollars which John C. Johnson is
hereby compelled to pay to Dorcas Hughes for which reason I do give and
bequeath all my stock of hogs, horses and all my household & kitchen
furniture, together with all the debts now owing to me and all the crop
and plantation tools and every other individual except 25 cents I do
give and bequeath unto my son Joseph Johnson which John C. Johnson is to
pay to Joseph Johnson twenty-five cents and because I gave him half of
thelandon which he now lives, and I do hereby constitute and ordain my
son John C. Johnson and Moses Matherton Executors to this my last will
and testament.  In witness whereof I have hereto set my hand and Seal
this third day of February in the year of our Lord One Thousand Eight
Hundred and Twenty-one

                                                             Her

       Signed and sealed and acknowledged                Ann X Johnson Seal)
       to be the last will and testament                     Mark
       in the presence of, - Attest
          A. Millikin
          John Gillet

       State of Tennessee         Will probated 15 October 1825
       Cocke County

         [Above copied by Lucile Dorroh Burton from records in Tuscaloosa
         County, Alabama, in 1962, for Z. F. Shelton, who furnished a copy
         to Shirlev Faucette.  See notes on Paqe 104.1

          NOTES BY Z. F. SHELTON CONCERNING ANN JOHNSON


          The above Ann Johnson was the wife of Benjamin Johnson, Jr.,
who died in 1805 in Richland Co. 5.C.  On Sept. 3, 1805 Ann and her
daughter Unity were granted letters of administration on Benjamin
Johnson's estate and Unity Johnson probably was born about 1784 to
have been of age to act as administrator and was evidently the eldest
and only one of the children old enough to act as administrator,
Will Book D.

          According to Mr. Steadman Ann and Benjamin Johnson apparently
had 9 children shown in the census but if so, all but the five named
above had died without issue before 1821.

          Soon after 3 Sept. 1805, Unity Johnson married - - - Brent
and her last named son, Thomas Brent was born in S.C. 6-12-1812 was
the one who married 10-22-1835 to Amelia Bell in Tuscaloosa Co. Ala.,
and were the parents of "Addie" Brent (m Brotherton), and "Addie" and
my grandmother Julia Ann Fox (Gregg) visited each other and called
each other "cousin" because "Addie's" grandmother Unity Johnson Brent
was a sister to Julia Annls grandmother Dorcas Johnson Hughes.  Unity
Johnson's sister Elizabeth also married a Brent first and Brent died
in S.C. and Elizabeth married Thos. Fox after they moved to Cocke
County, Tenn.  The Court Clerk of Cocke Co. advised me their records
burned "several years ago".

                                      Z. F. Shelton - 1962


(Will of Ann Johnson copied by Lucile Dorroh Burton, Granddaughter
of Emily Gregg and Joel Dorroh.  Emily Gregg was a sister to my
nrnfather John T. Greaa.  Z. F. Shelton. 1962)

            Exhibit 14                                                                105









                                                     FOX FAMILY CEMETERY


                            On homestead o Henry Fox
                            (1768-1852), eldest son of William
                            and Sarah Carroll Fox, and his
                            wife Sarah Harrell Fox (1772-1848).
                            Natives of Richland County, S.C.,
                            both descended from families
                            prominent in Virginia and the
                            Carolinas.  Moving to Tennessee
                            about 1814, thence to Tuscaloosa
                            County, Ala., Henry and Sarah
                            settled here about 1834, and
                            eight of their thirteen children
                            settled in the state.


                            Placed by Their Descendants                                            |
                                         1960






            The above is the text of a historical marker placed by the descendants
            of Henry and Sarah Harrell Fox, who since 1955 have met for an annual
            reunion at or near Hugh White State Park, Grenada, Mississippi.  The
            regular reunion date is the second Sunday of October.

Exhibit 2                                                                     110



                       CHART GIVES ANNE WEST'S FAMILY LINE

                              by Elizabeth H. Smith


          [Quoted from 'IRichmond Times-Dispatchll, Sunday, Dec. 10, 1950]


               Just acquired by the Virginia Historical Society is a unique
     genealogical chart in the form cf a peacock, showing the lineage of
     Anne West from King John of England and 13 of the Barons of Runnymede.
     Anne was the wife of the Hon. Henry Fox, Burgess nf King William County
     from 1712 until 1714, the year of his death.

               The eye of each of the 24 tail feathers of the peacock is the
     coat of arms, emblazoned in color, of the family whose line is traced    
     down the feather to Anne. The lines given are five from King John to     N
     Anne and 19 from the 13 barons to her. The barons are Richard de Clare,  i‹
     Earl of Clare and of Gloucester; John de Lacie, seventh Baron Helton     ‹
     and Earl of Lincoln, Roger Bigod, second Earl of Norfolk; William,       ‹
     Baron de Lanvillei;Henry, Baron de Bohun, Earl of Hereford; Saire de
     Quincey, Baron Quincey, Earl of Winchester; Robert de Vere, third Earl
     of Oxford; William, Baron de Bowbray, Lord Bowbray, of Alexholm, in
     Lincolnshire; Robert Baron FitzWalter, third Lord Dunmow (leader of the
     barons); Gilbert de Clare; Hugh Bigod, third Earl of Norfolk; William
     d'Albini, Lord of Belvoir; Robert, Baron Roos of Hamlake Manor.

                          Chart Made by Mrs. Fox                              

               The chart, 27 by 35 inches, and framed in mahogany and gilt,   |
     was presented to the Society of Descendants of the Hon. Henry Fox and
     Anne West, his wife, by the artist, Mrs. Ann Pritchard Woodard Fox, of
     Washington, D.C., honorary president of the society and widow of Edward
     Lansing Fox, a descendant of Ann and Henry Fox. She organized the
     society in 1934 and was its first president, serving for five years.     i
     The artist, who was born May 12, 1869, was 80 when she executed the
     chart. A grand daughter of Colonel Richard Ware Wyatt, of Albemarle
     County, she is a descendant of that early Jamestown divine, Rev. Hawte
     Wyatt, of Boxley, England.                                               ;

               At the meeting of the Society of Descendants of Anne and
     Henry Fox October 15, in the home of Judge and Mrs. J. Douglas Mitchell,
     Walkerton, the organization presented the chart to the Virginia Histori-
     cal Society. On behalf of Clayton Torrence, director and corresponding
     secretary of the Virginia Historical Society, George H. S. King, of
     Fredericksburg, accepted the gift, reading a letter to him from Mr.      
     Torrence in which Mr. Torrence said, in part:                            I

               "Most emphatically we wish to have this remarkable piece of    |
     work at the Virginia Historical Society. Place will be made for it,
     preferably somewhere in the Lee House. Mr. Herbert A. Claiborne,


                                                             1 1 1


Chart Gives Anne West's Family Line Continued)


chairman of the Lee House committee, the committee that has charge in
the matter of accepting gifts offered to the society, endorses whole-
heartedly the acceptance of this gift.  I delegate you to receive the
chart for us.'i

                       Three West Governors

          The chart, which has been placed in the library of the Vir-
ginia Historical Society, not only traces Anne West's ancestry for
approximately five centuries, but also lists her descendants for several
generations to about 1750.

          The English ancestry of Anne is also that of the three West
brothers who at different times served as early Governors of Virginia.
The brothers were Anne's grandfather, Captain John West, who was Gover-
nor from 1635 to 1637; Sir Thomas West, Third Lord de la Warr, first
resident Governor, 1609-1618, and Captain Francis West, who was Gover-
nor, 1627-1629. These brothers were sons of Sir Thomas West, Second
Lord de la Warr, and Anne Knollys, his wife, the daughter of Sir Francis
Knollys, and his wife, Lady Catherine Cary.  Lady Catherine was daughter
of Lady Mary Boleyn Cary, who was sister of Queen Anne, wife of Henry
VIII.

          About 1620 Captain John West came to Virginia, where he took
an active part in the affairs of the colony.  He was a member of the
King's Council from 1630 to 1659, the year of his death.  For some time
he was president of the council, Virginia's West Point was named for
him.

          Anne's husband, Henry Fox, was for many years a vestryman and
warden in St. John's Parish, King and Queen County, later King William.
He was first sheriff of the county, a highly respected position in that
day.  Previously he had been a magistrate in King and Queen in 1699.
Mr. King pointed out in an article in Tyler's Quarterly for April 1940,
that in 1702 the Honorable Henry Fox was appointed senior presiding
justice of King William County.  This was the year that that portion of
King and Queen County which was known as Pamunkey Neck became King
William.  Anne's father, Colonel John West, of York County, and her
maternal grandfather, Major Joseph Croshaw, also of York County, were
members of the House of Burgesses.  In 1702 Henry Fox and Richard Little-
page, of King William County, gave to the county two acres of land as
the site of a courthouse.  It is on part of this land that the courthouse
now stands.

          As history students well know, the Barons of Runnymede were a
group of 25 noblemen who on June 15, 1215, forced King John of England
to sign the document which is known as Magna Charta, its most important
article providing that no freeman shall be taken or imprisoned or pro-
ceeded against except by the lawful judgment of his peers or by the law

                                                                    1 1 2


         Chart Gives Anne West's Family Line (Continued)


         of the land and providing also that no scutage or aid shall be imposed
         in the kingdom (except certain feudal dues from tenants of the crown),
         unless by the common council of the kingdom.  This document, which is a
         carefully guarded treasure in the British Museum, forms the basis of
         practically all democratic law in the Western World.

                                 Scion of Conqueror

                   Of the 25 Barons who brought ing John to terms when they
         overtook him on the meadows of Runnymede and pledged themselves to see
         that its terms were carried out, 17 have known descendants.

                   The information on the West chart makes connection with other
         data which shows Anne West to be a descendant of William, the Conqueror,
         hero of the Battle of Hastings in 1066, and of Charlemagne, born in 742,
         whose far-flung domains were known as the Holy Roman Empire.

                   In discussing the chart:  The registrar of the Society of the
         Descendants of the Honorable Henry Fox and Ann West, his wife, said:
         "I was in Washington in the Spring when the peacock was displayed at
         the National Genealogical Society and it attracted much attention
         amongst the many professional genealogists and heraldic artists there."

                   Officers of the Society of Descendants, which includes member-
         ship from many parts of the United States, are president, Edward Jackson
         Fox, Silver Springs, Md.; vice-president, Judge Leon M. Bazile, Elmont;
         secretary, Mrs. J. Douglas Mitchell, Walkerton; treasurer, Mrs. John
         Thomas Lynch, Arlington; registrar, George H. S. King, and honorary
         president, Mrs. E. L. Fox, Washington, D. C.


             [Accompanying the above article was a picture of the "peacock",
             which bore the following cut line:

             "This chart, in the form of a peacock, traces the lineage of
             Anne West, wife of Henry Fox, Virginia Colonial burgess, from
             King John and 13 of the barons of Runnymede."]




             A photo of this article as it appeared in the "Richmond Times-
             Dispatch" was given to Shirley Faucette by Dr. J. Ben Robinson,
             with the following note:

                   "This peacock chart is now in the rooms of the Virginia
             Historical Society.  The artist, Mrs. Ann W. Fox, was a very
             close friend of mine.  The news item concerning it came into
             my possession through Mrs. Fox.  I had it mounted and photo-
             graphed in order to make copies available to the Fox clan.  I
             am happy to present this copy to you.      J.B.R.
             P.S.  You are eligible for membership in the Magna Charta Dames.

                  FOX FAMILY REUNION GROUP - MISSISSIPPI


               The following have served as President of the Fox Family Reunion
     group since its first reunion held in 1955.  Indicated in parentheses is
     the name of the son or daughter of Henry and Sarah Harrell Fox, from whom
     each was descended.

              1956-1957   John Henry Fox, .       (William Fox)
              1958        James Calliway Fox       (Henry Fox, Jr.)
              1959-1960   James E. Fox             (Henry Fox, Jr.)
              1961-1963   Carmon E. Valentine      (Jacob Fox)
              1964-1965   John Henry Fox, Jr.      (William Fox)
              1966        James Herman Fox         (William Fox)
              1967-1968   A. V. Henry              (Jacob Fox)
              1969-1970   John Henry Fox, III      (William Fox)
              1971-1972   Donald E. Skelton        (Jacob Fox)

               In 1956, Mrs. Dot Fox Mallory of the Thomas Fox line was unani-
     mously elected as Honorary Life President.  She, along with James C. Fox
     and Carmon E. Valentine, were instrumental in organizing the first reunion.

               Serving as Secretary from the group's organization in 1955 until
     1965, was Myrtie Fox Taylor (Mrs. John L. Taylor), of the Henry Fox, Jr.,
     line.  Subsequent secretaries have been:  1966-1968, Kathryn Runge Lowry
     (William Fox); 1969-1970, Shirley Faucette (William Fox); and 1971-1972,
     Jeanette House Dorroh (Henry Fox, Jr.).

               Mrs. Eleanor Fox Ponder (William Fox) served as the first pub-
     licity chairman, and publicity was later handled in connection with the
     Historian's duties by Shirley Faucette.  Don Skelton was elected Publicity
     Chairman for 1969-1970, and Epton Valentine for 1971-1972.

               A Historical Committee was appointed by the President in 1955,
     composed of Mrs. Gladys Mallory Hester (Thomas Fox), Carl Fox, Jr., (Thomas
     Fox), and Shirley Faucette.  The committee later was composed of Zuela
     Freeman Shelton (Jacob Fox) and Shirley Faucette, with the latter serving
     as Historian until 1967, when she nominated Dr. W. D. McCain of the Unity
     Fox Pryor line, who had recently become interested in Fox genealogy.  Dr.
     McCain has served in this position since, with Shirley Faucette as
     Assistant Historian.  Dr. McCain has concentrated his attention on the
     descendants of Henry and Sarah Harrell Fox, while Shirley Faucette has
     collected data on the ancestors of this couple.

               The office of First Vice President was created in 1968, with
     the understanding that the holder would advance to the Presidency.
     John H. Fox, III, was elected to this office in 1968; Donald Skelton
     was elected in 1969; and James T. Newsom (HenrY Fox Jr.) in 1971.

                                OUTLINE OF
                        POSSIBLE LINEAGE OF HENRY FOX
                    Born 6-12-1768, Richland County, S.C.,
                    Died 1852, Webster County, Mississippi



     This outline is based primarily on material given to Shirley Faucette
     from research done over a period of years by Joseph E. Steadman, Sr.,
     Batesburg, South Carolina, and from Dr. J. Ben Robinson, Baltimore,
     Maryland, and compares their views where different.  The names of the
     first four generations are given largely from the pedigree chart for
     "Fox of Great Missenden" from the "Visitation of Buckinghamshire"
     made in the years 1566, 1575, and 1634, a publication of the Harleian
     Society of London, edited by W. H. Rylands (1909), Volume 58, pages
     59-60.  Information for Col. Henry Fox of South Carolina and his
     children is largely from Mr. Steadman, with additions furnished by
     Shirley Faucette for subsequent generations.  Where opinions differ
     considerablv for an item. the source is hnwn.

HENRY FOX (lst)  "Son and heir of Mr. John Fox, Dec'd" - "Henry Fox, Gentleman"
b ca 1650; d 1714, King William County, Virginia
m ca 1673 to Anne West (b ca 1655-57; d after 1708), daughter of Col. John West and Unity Croshaw, daughter
of Major Joseph Croshaw of York County, Virginia.

He patented 300 acres in Gloucester and New Kent Counties, Va., 16 April 1782/3.  Lived most of adult life,
after 1690-95 in St. John's Parish on Pamunkey Neck, (King and Queen Co., now King William Co., Va.)  His
brick home was "Huntington" on the Mattaponi River.  He and Richard Littlepage donated two acres of land for
site of King William Court House, its present site.  Vestryman 1695 in St. John's Parish, King & Queen Co.,
Va., (now King William); justice of same county in 1699, King & Queen having been taken from Gloucester
County.  Commissioned 2-7-1702/3 as sheriff of King William County, and served as senior presiding justice, a
highly respected position in that day.  A plaque in his memory is in the courtroom of King William Court House
and also at St. John's Church.  Member of House of Burgesses from King William County from 1712 to 1714, date
of his death.  His wife's father, Col. John West (b 1632, d 1691) was son of Hon. John West, Governor of Vir-
ginia, the 12th child of Sir Thomas West, Second Lord De La Warr, with noble lineage back to King John of
England and 14 of 25 Barons of Runnymeade.

HENRY FOX (2nd)
b ca 1674-76; d after 1740, ca 1750
m-l ca 1697 to Mary Kendrick, b ca 1678, dau of William Kendrick of Abingdon Parish, Gloucester Co., Va.;
sister of John Kendrick.
m-2 ca 1705 to Mary Claiborne, b ca 1680, dau of William and Elizabeth Sudman Claiborne.  Also shown as
"probably sister of William Claiborne of Romancoke, King William Co., Va."  [Some sources list both wives;
others show only one, but vary as to whether it was Mary Kendrick or Mary Claiborne.]

Now generally accepted as son of Henry and Anne West Fox.  Recorded as living in St. Paul's Parish, Hanover
Co., Va., 1711-12-16 (formerly New Kent County); moved to King William Co., Va., sometime between 1716 and
1719.  In King William County:  deeded land to John Kendrick in 1721; was sheriff, 1724-25; Justice of
Peace in 1726; vestryman of St. John's Parish in 1730.  Patented 1000 acres of land in Spottsylvania Co.,
Va., in 1728, which he sold to Samuel Henderson in 1730, and in the same year sold other lands belonging to
him in Hanover County.

Moved to Brunswick Co., Va., in 1730.  Dr. Robinson states that Brunswick County records indicate Henry Fox
2nd located on the Meherrin River near Fort Christiana, near Lawrenceville, the present county seat of Bruns-
wick County, which he sold 3-18-1734/35 and may have moved to N.C. or S.C.  However, Mr. Steadman feels that
this was property of his son Henry 3rd, who went to S.C., and that Henry 2nd died in Virginia.  Also, Mr.
Steadman does not think that Richard Fox who owned land in this area belonged to this family;  Dr. Robinson
and Mr. J. R. Hendrick (a descendant of Richard Fox) list him as a son of Henry Fox 2nd.  In newly created
Brunswick County, Henry 2nd was a justice in 1732, continuing as Chief Justice of the Commission of the Peace
until 1734; was a member of the House of Burgesses in 1732-1734, said to have been the first member from Bruns-
wick Co.; was on committee of St. Andrew's Parish in 1732 to select site of chapel on South Side of Meherrin
River.

HENRY FOX (3rd) - "Col. Henry Fox of South Carolina"         [Information in this and succeeding pages of
b ca 1698-1702 in New Kent Co., Va. ?;  d 1768-1770, S.C.    this material, is largely from Mr. Joseph E.
m-l ca 1720/21 to Mary (Goodwyn?), (b ---; d after 1734)     Steadman and from research.]
m-2 8-20-1738 to Mrs. Martha Keen (widow)

Probably left King William County, Va., 1720-21, and moved to Brunswick County, Va.  Removed from Brunswick
Co., Virginia, to South Carolina some time between 1730 and 1734.  His petition for land in 1754 states that
he had been in S.C. about 20 years.  On 3-18-1735 Henry Fox of the province of North Carolina sold 555 acres
of land in Brunswick Co., Va., which adjoined land of John Carroll.  [Dr. Robinson is of the opinion that
the 555 acres was sold by Henry Fox 3rd's father, Henry Fox 2nd.]  Henry 3rd was then living in S.C. in a
section of the Welsh Tract, on Pee Dee River, claimed by both N.C. and S.C.  It appears that on 5-20-1731 he
was appointed petit juror for Prince Frederick Parich, Winyah, shortly after coming to S.C.  On 1-16-1735,
650 acres of land were surveyed for him in Craven Co., Prince George Parish, Winyah, the grant being signed
on 12-17-1737, the land being situated in the present Marlboro County, S.C., adjoining land granted to
Thomas Hume and Thomas Groom.  These three men (Henry Fox, who was also known as "Col. Henry Fox", Thomas
Hume and Thomas Groom) were described as "land grabbers" because of the large holdings of land which they
acquired.  Henry Fox is also referred to as l'an eminent lawyer in Charleston.ll During the latter part of
1742 or early part of 1743, he moved to Charleston, where for many years he was active in military affairs
of S.C.  In 1754 and 1764 Henry Fox obtained grants for lands in that part of Craven County, S.C., which is
now in Richland County, these lands being situated in the southeastern corner of the county in the fork of
the Wateree River and the Congaree River.

Henry Foxls wife was shown as IIMaryll in a deed given by him when he sold his lands in 1735.  It is believed
by Mr. Steadman that this was Henry Fox 3rd and that she was a Miss Goodwyn.  Parish records for Prince
Frederick Parish, Winyah, show the marriage of Henry Fox to Mrs. Martha Keen, Widow, on 8-20-1738.  It is
possible that she was the widow of Martin Keane who left property in that area to his wife Martha.

Children:
Unity Fox, b ca 1724; living 1778; m ca 1742 to Claudius Richbourg.  Three sons.
Thomas Fox, b 1725 in Va.; d 11-16-1822; m ca 1768 to Elizabeth Hancock.  Seven children. [Ancestor of JES.]
Henry Fox, b 1727; living 1759-60.
John Fox, b 1727-29; d 1781; m Mary ---; settled in Abbeville District, S.C.  Five children named in wifels will.
William Fox, b 1736-43; d 1816, Richland Co., S.C.; m Sarah Carroll (d before 4-13-1808, which was date of
Williamls will).  [See further.]
Temperance Fox, b 3-19-1i41
Joseph Fox, b 1745 (Sgt. in Revolution)

WILLIAM FOX - Of Richland County, South Carol ina
b ca 1743 (no definite date established for his birth)
d 1816 in Richland Co., S.C.  Will dated 4-13-1808; sworn to 6-20-1816; Will Book G.
m ca 1767 to Sarah Carroll (b ---; d before 4-13-1808)

Estimates of the date of William Foxls birth have ranged from as early as 1730 to as late as 1744, but cir-
cumstantial evidence indicates that he was probably born about 1743, the son of Henry Fox (3) and his
second wife, Mrs. Martha Keen Fox. See Section III of this book for further discussion of this and the
following items.

It is said that William Fox married l'his cousin" Sarah Carroll, according to tradition a niece and adopted
daughter of Charles Carroll.  Some have said this was Charles Carroll of Carrollton.  A will dated 1752
for Charles Carroll of Charleston names "the children of Martin Carroll by my sister Sarah of Droghadie,
Kingdom of Ireland".  No connection has been established for any Carroll.  It is speculated that she was a
sister of Jacob Carroll, Sr., who died in South Carolina 1810-15, (will listed six children, one of whom,
Unity Carroll m Joseph Collins).  The fact that Jacob Carroll named his daughter Unity, a frequent name in
the Fox family, may indicate that the "cousin" relationship between Sarah Carroll and her husband William
Fox was through the Fox line.

William Fox served as a soldier in the American Revolution under several Captains, being a horseman, cattle-
driver, and assistant to the Quartermaster.  Grants for land in the southeastern corner of Richland County
in the fork of the Wateree and Congaree Rivers, near lands of his father, Col. Henry Fox, were recorded in
1769 and 1770. His will indicates he was a well-to-do planter, owning at his death 706 acres of land and
ten slaves.  His twin sons Elijah and Elisha and daughter Unity Fox Carter are buried in the cemetery adjoin-
ing Good Hope Baptist Church, located east of Columbia, S.C., in the Middleboro community.

Sarah Fox ? (not named in fatherls will ) .

                      SECTION VI



"TOMBSTONE TOUR"                                     Page 125


   Some points of interest for the Fox Family in Virginia,
   South Carolina. and Miccissinni_

                                                                         125



  Introduction

       to

"TOMBSTONE TOUR"



          In July 1970, Mother and I made a trip through the Fox country
of Virginia and South Carolina.  It reminded us of the old poem which
says "The paths of glory lead but to the grave".  That's where most of
our paths led.  To cemeteries and church yards.

          Sometimes it is an old house or church you are looking for
but cannot find because time - or urban renewal - was there just before
you arrived.  Or, as Dr. McCain once said, you plan to visit an old
friend and keep putting it off, you finally arrive just as the hearse
is leaving.  So we decided to visit as many of the old homes, court
houses, churches, and cemeteries as we could find, along with a few
live friends and relatives.

          We decided to follow my uncle's advice when he went to the
first Fox Reunion:  "If anyone says 'come in' when you yell 'tallyho'
you'll know you are in the Fox crowd!"

          Our first stop was a mission for Mr. Jasper R. Hendrick, or
"Cousin Jerry", now of Abilene, Texas.  He was in Virginia several years
doing research on Foxes, but moved to Texas before completing his work.
Before we left home he had written and called to make sure I was properly
rehearsed for a visit to Mooresville, Alabama, where, he hoped, a Mrs.
Zeitler had just the proof he was looking for on his family.  I was to
try to find her, though she had answered no one's letters for several
years.  No telephone was listed for Mrs. Zeitler, either, and I was
afraid I was headed toward the cemetery.  But just before we left I got
a call telling me her name was not Zeitler after all; she had remarried
and I was to look for Mrs. Henry Willis Hill.  We found Mrs. Hill.  She
lived in a lovely colonial-style home in the center of town, and she was
delighted to talk about ancestors, but did not correspond any more because
of trouble with her eyes.

          I never convinced her I was not trying to join a historical
society; she belonged to all of them.  All I wanted was to find for Cousin
Jerry where she found information concerning Richard Fox on Page 1 of
Order Book 1 of the Brunswick County, Virginia, records that she gave as
reference on the applications of her daughters in the Colonial Dames.  She
could remember every date and every person she had ever talked with except
that.  She only remembered a "researcher" had given it to her.  We later
learned that the original had been missing for some time prior to 1916,
and although a copy had been made by Mrs. J. 0. James, a Fox descendant,
before its loss, the copy did not refer to Richard.  So Cousin Jerry still
does not have the Droof he wanted.

                                                                         126



          But we did not leave empty handed.  Mrs. Hill had a small note-
book into which she had copied by hand a history of an allied Fox family,
written by a Mrs. Peck in 1894.  She finally suggested, after much agony
on my part that she would not consider it, that I could take the note
book to copy and return it by mail.  We thanked her sincerely, and left
quickly before she could change her mind.  Later, others wanted to
snatch it from me, but I managed to hold on to it long enough to make
copies before returning it to Mrs. Hill.

          We met Cousin Jerry in 1971, when we saw him and other Fox
descendants in Abilene.  He sent me his picture which appeared in the
Abilene papers on his 86th birthday in 1970 - a light-hearted, spirited,
young 86!  He is still searching for proof that his ancestor, Richard
Fox, was a son of Henry 2, and a brother of our Colonel Henry Fox
(Henry 3).  He promised to come serenade me with his ukulele if I find
the proof!  He's in there going strong and is the sparkplug of Abilene
activities ranging from the Hendrick Home for Children to the Chess                    il
Club, with ancestors thrown in just for good measure.  We claimed him
as a cousin, Order Book 1, or not!

          In Baltimore, we spent a lovely day with Dr. J. Ben Robinson.
He's a charming, vivacious, and brilliant gentleman, and we claimed                    ||
another cousin. He told us he was 88 then, and he is in his prime!                     il
Dr. Robinson was singularly honored at the ovember 1970 meeting of the                l
American Dental Association by receiving the Award of Excellence of the
Aerican College of Dentists the only such award given in the ACD's                   ;
fifty year history) for outstanding service to the dental profession and
 the public. Dr. Robinson helped establish the West Virginia School of                  .
 Dentistry and is Dean Emeritus of his Alma Mater, the University of
 Maryland's Baltimore College of Dental Surgery.                                        -

           From Dr. Robinson we left with another treasure - his typed
 manuscript of facts and suppositions about the Foxes, which he calls "A
 Guide to Further Study of the Fox Family of Virginia".  Dr. Robinson
 hopes to publish this material himself, but he did lend it to me, and
 let me make a copy.  He is not yet through with his research, and Fox is
 only one of many lines on which he is working.  He did say I could quote
 from his work, provided I specify that it includes opinions.

           Most "Fox" opinions vary!  Dr. Robinson, Mr. Hendrick, Mr.
 Steadman, and George H. S. King have apparently had some interesting
 correspondence concerning their differing opinions.

           For instance, if you've done much reading about the Foxes, you
 may have noticed articles written by George H. S. King.  We've missed
 meeting him!)  Mr. King is a recognized Fox Family Historian, with many
 published articles, and he has been registrar of the organization of
 "The Society of Descendants of Henry Fox and Anne West", which meets
 in Virginia on the second Sunday each October - the same date the Missis-
 sippi Foxes hold their reunion.  For many years our Henry Fox (2) was not
 recognized by that group as being a son of Henry and Anne West Fox.  How-

                                                                         127



 Robinson in 1965, "I am willing to accept him as such...."  Dr. Robinson
 and Mr. Steadman both quote Mr. King, but also give their own differing
 opinions, and differ on some points with each other.

           Finally, we met Mr. Joseph E. Steadman, Sr.  He is the closest
 "cousin" to the Mississippi Foxes of these mentioned, being also descended
 from Col. Henry Fox of South Carolina.  We have been corresponding since
 1955.  Margaret Glen Fox Brown had written to him earlier, and he quotes
 correspondence from Cousin Bessie Fox Truly in 1917.  Cousin Herman Fox
 tried to see him when Mr. Steadman was living in Washington before moving
 back to South Carolina, and my uncle, John H. Fox, Jr., tried to locate
 him at Batesburg, but none of these ever met.

           We arranged to spend nearly a day with Mr. Steadman at his
 home between Saluda and Batesburg.  He, too, is a dear man, gracious,
 brilliant, and meticulous in his work.  He is now retired after having
 worked and lived for many years in Washington, D.C.  He studied originally
 for the ministry, but had to go into other work because of a throat diffi-
 culty.  I wished for a photographic memory as we visited.  I didn't leave
 with his book, though I tried!  He brought out to show me the manuscript
 he had been working on for years.  Pages and pages, legal size, written
 in delicate writing, with supplemental sheets inserted.  I had time to
 make only a few notes; they made my research at the South Carolina
 Archives much simpler.  I offered to type his manuscript and return it to
 him for corrections, but he preferred to type it himself so he could check
 it.  He did promise that when he finished, he would give it to me to use in
 whatever way I wished.

           It was in July 1970 that Mr. Steadman promised me his material.
 At Christmas he wrote that he hoped to start typing soon.  In August 1971,
 I received another letter saying he was recovering from an illness; he was
 sending the manuscript for me to type.  I promised I would sleep with it
 under my pillow until completed!  The result is the fascinating book which
 comprises the first part of this volume.

           Unless you have spent days searching and found not even one
 small clue, you cannot imagine what monumental work these men have given
 me.  Years and years of work by painstaking researchers given free.  Pro-
 fessional genealogists charge for searching - not finding.  These men
 have done most of the work themselves, because they enjoyed it, but they
 have also paid researchers and had other costs.  Yet, this comes as a
 free gift.

           We can only say, "Thanks, Cousins!"

Clinton, Mississippi
