                                                             1 28




                        "TOMBSTONE TOUR"


VIRGINIA:

          As we came into the Virginia peninsulas where the 17th
century English, including our Foxes, first settled, no one was sur-
prised when we stopped to ask "Which way to the cemetery?" -- or "How
do you get to Ware Church?"--and "Do you know about an old home
named 'Retreat'?" Women working in their flowers stopped to visit and
invited us in for a cup of coffee.  The Court House and jail were
nearly always in the center of town, and we made the acquaintance of
several police chiefs and city clerks along the way.  If the Police
Chief could not tell us about Ware Church, for instance, he would call
up the newspaper editor that just happened to be interested in his-
tory; not only did the editor of the Gloucester, Virginia, paper tell
us where Ware Church was, he and his family took their car, went with
us, and found the tombstone we were hunting -- then took our picture
with the tombstone and published it on the front page of "Glo-Quips",
the paper of Gloucester County.

Williamsburg:

          A good place to start a Fox Tour is from Williamsburg.  Walk-
ing down the streets of Williamsburg, you can remember that Henry Fox
(first and second) attended meetings of the House of Burgesses here.
In Bruton Church you can see the plaque in honor of Joseph Croshaw,
whose daughter Unity was the mother of Anne West, wife of Henry Fox.
Three West brothers, including Anne's grandfather, Capt. John West,
served as early governors of Virginia.  In the wig-maker's shop you can
imagine how the shop of Charles Carroll, whose 1752 will says he was a
"peruke maker", might have looked in Charleston.  (You can also go to
Jack Sharp's rare book shop on Duke of Gloucester St.  It's fun, but I
think we got the last copy he had of "Fox Trails in Old Virginia"!)

York County:

          Look out over the river and bay at Yorktown and remember
Captain John Fox (there may have been both a father and a son by this
name) who was a skilled navigator and commander of various ships belong-
ing to English trading companies.  When Charles II was proclaimed King
of England in 1660, the Governor of Virginia declared his allegiance to
the Crown and ordered all the counties of the colony to do likewise at
"Your uttermost peril."  The celebration in York County was described
as follows:  See Page 10 of Mr. Steadman's book.]

    "The restoration of Charles to the throne was celebrated in
    Virginia, on proclamatinon of the governor....  The 'William
    and John' was in port during the celebration and Captain John
    Fox, Commander, participated in the occasion.  The York County
    Levy, October 1660, included a sum to pay Captain Fox for 6
    cases of drams [Webster:  "A small portion of something to drink"]
    and for firinq his nreat gunnes'."



                                                                         129




Gloucester County:

           From Yorktown you can cross the York River to Gloucester Point.
Gloucester County is where Captain John Fox first owned land.  About 11
miles north is the town of Gloucester.  We stopped at Abingdon Parish
Church along the way.  We found no Fox tombstones, but parish records
show the names of many of the Fox family.

           At Gloucester we were looking for Ware Church.  Our special
interest was in finding the tomb of Isabell Booth Fox, wife of the Rev.
John Fox, born in 1706.  Though Dr. Robinson and Mr. Steadman disagree
on his exact relationship in the family, he did belong--somewhere--in
our Fox family, and had his family coat of arms inscribed on the tomb
of his wife.  At that time you used a Coat of Arms only if you were
entitled to it--not because you ordered one you liked from a catalog.
The arms on her tomb have been identified as those of the Fox Family of
Great Missenden, Buckinghamshire, England.

           Great Missenden, Buckinghamshire, England, was an ancient
village about 30 miles north of west from the old City of London, and
the church was founded there in 1133.  In 1634 there was a "Visitation"
of the County of Buckingham by the Royal College of Arms, which shows
that in the south window of the Chancel of the Parish Church were the
arms of a Fox Family placed there between 1602 and 1626.  It is under-
stood from research that the insignia itself is older than the College
of Arms, founded in 1464.  For additional information see Parts I and
II of Mr. Steadman's book.

           l'e thought it would be simple to take a picture of the tomb
to add to our collection.  Several reference books gave the inscription
from the tomb and described the coat of arms.  It was "beneath the
chancel" of Ware Church.  We really didn't have any trouble finding
Ware Church; we found it by ourselves and the caretaker let us drive
through the cemetery and go inside the church.  We looked all over the
sanctuary, including the choir loft, for the tombs.  We found nothing
but red carpet.  That was when we went back looking for someone who
knew about Ware Church and were referred to Mr. William DeHardit,
editor of the Gloucester newspaper, "Glo-Quips".  Mr. DeHardit was a
member of Ware Church and enlightened us.  Not all the books carry the
full note:  "Rev. Jno. Fox and his wife's tombstones are under the
chancel of Ware Church.  In 1854 the floor oF the chancel was extended
over the tombs."  He also had other information.  A young woman of the
church had been interested in the tombs, and had lain on the ground in
the crawl space under the floor and made a silk rubbing of the big flat
monument.  The rubbing was mounted on plywood and stored in the base-
ent of the Parish House, and it was here that the DeHardits took us
an made our picture standing by the rubbing of the tombstone.  It came
!Ut on the front page of that week's edition.

           The Court House and tiny jail at Gloucester are on the square
in the center of town.  In 1769 the Governor of Virginia approved an
act aLIt    z na John Fox. "aentleman" (which one I do not know) to lay

                                                                         130



off 60 acres of his land, adjoining the lands on which the courthouse
of Gloucester is erected, for a town to be called Botetourt Town in
honor of the Governor of the colony.

          The earliest date at which our Captain John Fox is known to
have owned land in Virginia and upon which he lived at the time was in
1667, his land being on both sides of Poropotanke Creek, which was the
line that separated the two counties of Gloucester and New Kent.  From
New Kent County came King & Queen Count;y in 1691, and from King & Queen
came King William in 1701.  Poropotank  River is the dividing line
between Gloucester County and the southern part of the present King &
Queen County, and is just south of West Point, where the Mattaponi and
Pamunkey Rivers converge to form the York River.

          Probahly Captain John Fox owned other land, but the only
record is of this particular tract on which his "son and heir" Henry
Fox later applied for a patent.  Henry, the "son and heir", we call
"Henry l" because Henrys 2 and 3 follow in a straight line in our family.
Also, it is easy to designate this one as Henry that married Anne West.
Some of the wives we are not sure of, but Anne West we know about--all
the way back to Adam, if you look at the "Adam Chart" which Jerry Hen-
drick sent me!

          Articles on the West Family are numerous.  See Chapter 2 of
Part III of Mr  Steadman's book, and also Exhibit 2 of Section V of
this material.  Margaret McNeill Ayres of Memphis was the editor of "The
Noble Lineage (If the Delaware-West Family of Virginia Through Col. John
West, his sons and his daughter, Anne West who Married Henry Fox", bv
Ann Woodard Fox, published in 1958.  A letter dated October 1970 fro;
Mrs. Ayres's dughter, Mary Margaret Hill (Mrs. M. M. Hill), 623 So.
McLean, Memphis, Tenn., 38104, advised that Mrs. Ayres died in April
1970, but that some copies of her book were then available at $10 from
her.  This is an excellent book on the West ancestry.  An article by
George S. H. ing, "Memorial to Henry Fox, Gentleman, of 'Huntington',
Kjng William (ounty, Virginia," is contained in Tyler's Quarterly,
Volume 21, pages 217-290.

King William County:

          West Point - As you enter King William County from the south,
you come first to West Point, named in honor of the family of Anne West.
It was of special interest to us that Anne West's father, Col. John West
was honored by the council of Virginia in 1632 for being "the first
Christian child born on the York River"; and again, in 1660, by the
House of Burgesses, with a resolution of good will in recognition of the
services of the West family  exempting him from payment of taxes during
the remainder of his life. See Mr. King's article on pages 184-185 of
Mrs. Avrt?s's hnnk refPrrPtl t_n hnvP.l

                                s , Sweet Hal l , Romancoke: Henry Fox (l )
      probably spent most of his adlt lfe r key Neck area of King
      William County. This area has been in three different counties, but is
      now in King William County. George H. S. King says that Henry's home
      "Huntington" was on the Mattaponi River, and that it was a beautiful                     3
      brick home, with octagonal shaped bricks. In his 1939 article he says
      "The brick walls were standing until a few years ago." [See Tyler's                      |
      article referred to above.] A brick from this home is in a collection
      of bricks by the late Rev. Arthur P. Gray of West Point, Virginia. No
      tombstones are said to be at the location of the family cemetery at the
      former site of "Huntington". Also in ing William County [see map in
      this section] were "Fox's", the home of Henry's brother, Thomas Fox,                     I_
      (ancestor of Mr. King) which Mr. King described in 1939 as being in a
      "deplorable state of repair". A drawing of this home is contained in
      Mrs. Ayres's book. A photograph which shows its deplorable state is
      contained as the frontispiece of "Some Fox Trails in Old Virginia",
      compiled by Ellen M. Cocke in 1939. She describes it: "'Fox's' on the
      Mattaponi River, Built by Either Henry Fox (1650-1714) or by his Son
      Thomas Fox, Who Received a Royal Grant on the Mattaponi in 1713." Mrs.
      Cocke also describes "Sweet Hall", the home of Anne Fox Claiborne,
      (Sister of Henry Fox and wife of Captain Thomas Claiborne) "'Sweet Hall '
I     is still standing--a very quaint and interesting house." "Romancoke"
!     was the home of William Claiborne, Secretary of Virginia, who was the
      grandfather of Thomas Claiborne of "Sweet Hall", and may have been a
      kinsman of Mary laiborne, wife of Henry Fox (2). (We did not stop to
      hunt these houses or former sites, as we entered King William County
I     from the north, and ran out of time.)

                King William Courthouse: Henry Fox (l) was the first we know
      of in a long line of Foxes who held many public offices in their local
      areas. In 1702 he and Richard Littlepage donated two acres of land for
      the site of the King William Courthouse, its present site. He was com-                    _
      missioned February 2, 1702/03 as sheriff, and he was a Justice of the
      Peace, the senior presiding Justice, a highly respected position at that
      time. A plaque at the Courthouse in King William Courthouse was placed
      by his descendants in 1952 back of the Judge's seat in the room where he
I     was first presiding Justice of this Court.

               The town, King William Courthouse, as is the case in many
 i'   counties of Virginia, was known by the name of the county, plus "court-
      house" (e.g., Appomatox Courthouse). We arrived there on an election day
      and stopped at the Clerk's office to see if we could go into the court-
      room where the election was being held, explaining that we were descended
      from Henry Fox. The clerk, Mr. Thomas T. H. Hill, was most gracious--and
      we made the acquaintance of another "cousin". He was a descendant of
      Anne Fox Claiborne, and was the Secretary of "The Society of Descendants
      of Henry Fox and Anne West".

                He took us on a tour of the courtroom, called our attention to
      the pattern of the brick work (we later learned it was "Flemish Bond"
      used in many colonial buildings), and posed for our movies while vigorously

                                                             1 32




discussing social problems in Mississippi and Virginia with Mother.  As
a memento, he gave us a booklet containing the history of the Court-
house, and a "King William County Medal", cast in 1957 as an independent
part of the King William County observance of the 250th anniversary of
the founding of Jamestown.  The medal depicts King William Courthouse,
with the inscription "Oldest Courthouse in Continuous Use".

          Retreat:  Before leaving King William County, try to visit
"Retreat".  It is not a must for our family, since we are not directly
connected with its builder, but it is interesting.  It is said to have
been the home of Captain John Fox (grandson of Thomas and Mary Tunstall
Fox), which he sold to his nephew James Fox in 1800.  [See Page 55 of
"Fox Trails in Old Virginia", referred to above.]

          We found "Retreat" after several stops to inquire.  From
Ay1ette, in King William County, go north on Highway 600 about ten miles
(past Beaulahville Church) and reach Highway 604.  Turn left at a sign
which says "To Duane".  Go to the old store and turn left again.  Narrow
roads wind through wooded sections, and about a mile off the road is a
drive to the right leading to "Retreat".  Its present owners were Capt.
and Mrs. J. D. Myers, a retired Army officer and his wife, who were in
the process of having it registered as a national land mark.  Later they
will probably charge admission, but they gave us a free tour as they
were still in the process of moving in and we were, I think, their first
"Fox" visitors.  The chimney bears a date at the top in big figures of
"1808", when James Fox made an addition to the original house.  By that
date four generations of our Foxes had been in South Carolina.

Hanover County:

          Old Church:  We missed "Old Church" in Hanover County, about
12 miles from St. John's Church in King William.  We will have to go back.
Here Henry Fox (2) probably lived in the early 1700's in St. Paul's
Parish, New Kent County, near the Lower Church, at what is now Old Church.
His estate was apparently in the vicinity of the estates of his uncles,
Nathaniel and Thomas West, and probably the land of all three was part of
a disputed inheritance from their father and grandfather, Col. John West.
Henry (2) was a vestryman at St. Paul's Parish at "ye Lower Church" in
March 17, 1711/12.  He was still living there in 1716, but he probably
returned to King William following his father's death in 1714, as he was
shown as absent from the Parish in 1719.

          Henry Fox (2) has been the subject of much debate as to whether
or not he was a son of Anne West and Henry Fox.  Dr. Robinson says:

    "A painstaking search of such fragmentary court records as remain...
    has not yielded a single piece of direct evidence that supports the
    claim of a father and son relationship between these two Henry Foxes
    ....  Therefore, a determination of the validity of the claim...
    must be based upon the probable reliability of a widespread family
    tradition, a rational interpretation of the true meaning of related
    circumstantial evidence, and the accuracy of the reasoning used by
    the investigator in making a final judgment."

                                                                                   133



                   Finally, in 1965, after much research, Dr. Robinson wrote:
          "Mr. George H. S. King, a well known genealogist and a recognized
I         authority on Fox family history has declared: 'I think it is for
          certain that he Henry Fox 2nd] was son of Henry and Anne (West) Fox
          and I am willing to accept him as such."'

                    In King William, Henry (2) followed in his father's footsteps
          and was twice appointed sheriff, in 1724 and in 1725.  There is no
          record of the date he was made a vestryman at St. John's church, but in
          May 1731, there is an entry that a new vestryman was chosen "in room of
          Mr. Henry Fox, who hath left the Parish".  He disposed of property in
          1730 in Hanover County and probably moved to Brunswick County that year.

          Brunswick County:

                    Here Henry (2) probably established his residence on the south
          side of the Meherrin River near Fort Christiana.  His son, Henry (3),
          known as Colonel Henry Fox, had already left King William County, and
          about 1720/21 moved to the newly formed Brunswick County shortly after
          his marriage to his first wife.  She was probably Mary Goodwyn; the
          Goodwyns settled in the adjoining Dinwiddie County and many later went
          to Richland County in South Carolina.

                    Mr. Steadman feels that in view of the later activity of Col.
          Henry (3) in connection with Indian affairs in South Carolina, he was
          probably associated in some way after he moved to Brunswick County with
          the Virginia Indian Company, which had control of all trade with the
          Indians south of the James River.

                    Fort Christiana:  In 1714 Governor Spotswood directed that
          this fort be built on the south bank of the Meherrin River, about two
          miles west of the present town of Lawrenceville, the present county seat
          of Brunswick County.  This fort was under the authority of a group of
          influential frontiersmen who formed the Virginia Indian Company.  There
          was an Indian village near the fort and the company operated a successful
          Indian school here.

                    How do youfind the site of Fort Christiana?  From the histori-
          cal markers along the highway you think it will be a state or national
          historic shrine.  The site probably had not been visited in years until
          we went.  A marker on Highway 1 reads "Nine miles south is the site of
          Fort Christiana, built in 1741," etc., and another reads "Three miles
          south is the site...."

                    We stopped at Brunswick Courthouse at Lawrenceville - I was
          sure both Henry 2 and 3 had been there - and inquired which way to the
          Fort.  They didn't remember.  Which way to Cochran?  (Where the first
          courthouse had been, and which was supposed to have been about six miles
          from where one of the Henry's owned land.)  Well, they said, there wasn't
          much at Cochran now.  (They were conservative; all we saw was the cross-
          roads, or else we missed it altogether.)  But they were busy washing the
          Sheriff's car and it was getting late, so I went across the street to the
          ten cent store.

 ;

                                                                         134



           The clerk had heard of Fort Christiana, but she did not know
how to get there.  A teen-age black boy, Robert Brown, was sweeping the
floor so they could close in a few minutes.  He knew where the Fort had
been.  He also warned there was not much there now--just some "old
cannons" down in the field, but he went home that way and would be
glad to show us the way.  It was after 5:30 and dusk was approaching.
He took off in his car--down a gravel road--and we followed.  From
Lawrenceville take Highway 46 South about three miles and turn right
on to Highway 686.  About a mile down the road turn off the gravel road
to the right and go down a dirt road about a mile.  There was a barbed
wire fence with a big brown gate, and a garbage dump.  No inscription.
No marker.  It was too late to walk down into the woods to hunt cannons.
There was no indication that this was the site described by all the
historical markers along the highways.  History makes rather prominent
mention of it as a fort, but the site is now an all-but-forgotten loca-
tion on a hilltop in Brunswick County, not far from the Meherrin River.
The Sheriff did not know where it was, and you reach it the hard way
and wonder how Henry Fox found it 20 years ago!

           Mr. Steadman says that doubtless it was here Henry (3) became
experienced in Indian affairs and military activities which gained for
him the title of "Colonel" in Brunswick County and later in South Caro-
lina.  Rev. John Fox (whom Mr. Steadman feels was Colonel Henry's brother;
Dr. Robinson places him as a nephew of Henry l) was a teacher in the
Indian School at William and Mary College prior to being ordained as a
minister.  It is also interesting that later, in Mississippi, Malachi Fox
is said to have spoken several Indian languages and was well thought of
by the Indians.  Eleanor Fox Ponder said that in 1908 she visited Mala-
chi's first cousins, James Denton and James Pryor; they told her of their
trip as assistants to Malachi Fox, who acted as a deputy in accompanying
the Indians moved from Mississippi to Indian Territory on the famed
"Trail of Tears".

           Back to Henry (2).  Brunswick was a new county, so you know
what happened.  They set up a court and ordered that a Commission of the
Peace be prepared.  The commission appointed ten persons--with whose name
first on the list as presiding Chief Justice?  Right.  Henry Fox (2).  He
also served in the House of Burgesses from 1732 to 1734.  He withdrew
from the Commission of Peace whose meetings he attended in June 1734),
and on August 10 the justices selected candidates for an expected vacancy
on the Commission and recommended that "Henry Fox, Gentleman, wait on the
Hon'l Governor with his recommendations."  [See Page 31 of Mr. Steadman's
book.]

           Dr. Robinson says:  "A thought for others to meditate:  'Did
Henry Fox (2) Gentleman, of Brunswick County, Virginia, finally locate in
South Carolina, as did his oldest son, Col. Henry Fox, who left Virginia
in 1733 and settled in South Carolina?'  Mr. Steadman says no; he thinks
Henry (2) died in Virginia.

           But all agree that Colonel Henry Fox (3) did go to South
Crnl in _

                                                                         135



SOUTH CAROLINA:

          When that 555 acres of land in Brunswick County (near Fort
Christiana) was sold in 1735, Henry Fox described himself as "of the
province of North Carolina", being then in a section on the Welch
tract which both North and South Carolina claimed at the time.  Mr.
Steadman thinks that this was Henry (3) and that he and his wife re-
turned from Carolina to Brunswick County for that purpose.  Henry Fox
signed the deed in person, witnessed by local persons.  The late Mrs.
J. 0. James, of Petersburg, Virginia, a descendant of William and Sarah
Avent Fox, found an item in a Brunswick County record which stated that
Mary Fox, the wife of Henry Fox had been unable to attend court and
sign the deed of transfer and that a comrnittee had been appointed to
secure her signature.  Here again, the record cannot now be found.

          The first settlements and first grants of land began about
that time, with settlers taking up land along the more important creeks.
Henry Fox (3) was granted land situated on Muddy Creek and it adjoined
the lands of Col. Thomas Hume and Thomas Groom.

          Colonel Henry Fox's second marriage was to Mrs. Martha Keen, a
widow, on August 20, 1738, recorded in the Register Book of Prince
Frederick Parish, Winyah, South Carolina.  Mr. Steadman is of the opinion
[see Page 27 of this collection] that William Fox was their son and that
he was born about 1743 at his father's plantation on or near the Black
River in the present Clarendon or Williamsburg County, moving about 1746
with his father to or near Charleston, South Carolina.

Charleston:

          A trip to this city is well worth it for anyone.  However, we
know of no specific location in the city with which Colonel Henry Fox or
his family, or the Carrolls, can be definitely connected at this time.

Richland County:

          Driving through the area where the Foxes lived described as the
confluence of the Wateree and Congaree Rivers,  there are few landmarks
for Foxes, but you can recall that the children and grandchildren of Henry
and William Fox lived in rnany locations.  Here William Fox and several
probable kinsmen were in some of the many battles of the American Revolu-
tion fought in South Carolina.  Fort Camden, a British stronghold, was
just north of his home, and battles were numerous.  It is believable that a
British soldier happened along to disturb Sarah Carroll Fox's churning!

          One of Henry Fox's plantation homes was on Second Creek (the
second creek above the confluence of the Congaree and Wateree Rivers),
which stream became known as Colonel's Creek in deference to the title of
Colonel Henry Fox.  [See Page 50 of Mr. Steadman's book.]  Mr. Steadman
also says that the location of this home apparently was near the mouth of
this creek and a short distance above the present Highway 378.

                                                                         136




          On this trip to Columbia we saw more of the microfilms, the
will books, and the land records at the Archives than we did of the
surrounding country, except for our trip to visit Mr. Steadman.  But
in addition to the known Fox haunts, we drove through Camden and saw
the figure on the weathervane on the clock tower which represents King
Haiglar, the friendly Chief of the Catawba Indians, who is sometimes
called the "patron saint" of Camden.  Just east of Columbia we stopped
on Highway 76 to read the markers at the former locations of "Woodland"
and "Millwood".  The first was the home built before 1800 by Wade
Hampton 1st (of the Revolutionary War), and the latter built before
1820 by his son, Wade Hampton 2nd, father of Wade Hampton 3rd, of the
Confederate Army.  Both homes were burned by Union troops in 1865, and
only six columns remain of Millwood.  We paid our respects, not only
because they were said to be friends and neighbors of William Fox, but
also because th? first Wade Hampton may have been a cousin or other
kinsman of my maternal grandmother's ancestor, Colonel Andrew Hampton
of the Revolutionary Army.

          Good ope Baptist Church:  In 1970 we had no trouble finding
again the laeboro Community, east of Columbia, on Highway 76, approxi-
mately 1.3 mils west of the intersection with Highways 601 and 378.  In
the cemetery of this church are tombstones for Unity Fox Carter, Elisha
and Elijah Fox  a sister and twir brothers of "Grandpappy Henry Fox
(1768-1852).  !'11 were children of William Fox, son of Colonel Henry (3).

          On a previous trip in 1960, we had driven over a large section
of this area, -including stops at Wateree, Congaree, Eastover, and Gadsden,
looking in eror) for "McLeod's Chapel near Goodhope Methodist Church",
where the abov!? graves were thouyht to be located.  At the Congaree
Baptist Church between Gadsden and Wateree, we found a cemetery with a
wrought iron fence around the Scott plot, but found they were buried
there in the 150's, much too recently  for our purposes.

          We stopped at Rutland's Grocery, the general store and post
office at Wateree, to mail a card to Uncle John with the Wateree post
mark.  The owner kindly called up "Miss Eleanor", who had lived in that
section a lon time to inquire about churches and cemeteries for us.
She directed us back to Highway 76 to find "McLeod's Methodist Church",
and gave us the name of C. B. Bowen's store, where we could inquire
further.  By chance, "Miss Eleanor's" husband, the rural mail carrier
whose name we forgot, came in while we were there and accompanied us to
McLeod's Methodist Church.  We searched every tombstone, but there were
no Foxes.  I shuffled my notes again and muttered something about l'Good
Hope".  The Good Hope Batist Church, he said, was just up the road.  lle
decided it was worth a try, and there we found the Foxes and the cemetery
we had searched for.  The church was in fair condition when we first saw
it in 1960.  It was a large white square frame church, with square wooden
pillows.  "Miss Eleanor's" husband told us he thought it was built before
the Civil War, and it looked as if it might have been.  Inside, it had a
'Iqallerv'' ar(ln two sides and at the bak.

       _



                                                                         137




           About 1963, a local resident told my uncle that the highway
 had been built over a portion of the cemetery.  Wonder if that was
 where William and Sarah Carroll Fox were buried?

           In 1970, Good Hope seemed to have suffered.  At the side of
 the church, next to the cemetery, appeared to be the trash from a
 church picnic some time ago, and the building itself, was showing the
 need for loving care.  Go quickly if you want to see it--it may not
 last much longer!

MISSISSIPPI:

           The final "Tombstone Tours" include several locations near
Slate Springs, as shown on portions of county maps in this section.
These include the family cemetery of Henry and Sarah Harrell Fox, and
the cemeteries at Bethany and Bethel Baptist churches.  All can be
Visited in half a day and are only a short drive from Grenada.

           Fox Cemetery:  Known graves at the cemetery on the former
homestead of Henry and Sarah Carroll Fox, in addition to theirs,
include those for their daughter Mary Fox Denton and her husband David
Denton; Jesse and Dorcas Johnson Hughes; John Shaw and his wife, Nancy.
There are other unmarked graves.

           Bethany Church Cemetery:  Others of Henry and Sarah Fox's
children buried here are Joseparroll Fox and his wife Margaret
Crossland Fox; James Thigpen Fox (his wife is buried in Denton, Tex. );
Jacob and Mary Ann Carolne Fox; Thomas and Elizabeth Johnson Brent Fox.

           Unity Fox Pryor and her husband, Samuel Odle Pryor are buried
in the Pryor Cemetery on a hill several miles southwest of Slate Springs,
near Lindsey Creek, in Webster County.  Dorcas Fox Mills is buried at
Shiloh Cemtery, ten miles west of Lewisville, in Denton County, Texas.

           It is not known where Henry Fox, Jr., and his wife are buried,
but a descendant, James C. Fox, surmised that it may have been at the
Fox Family cemetery.  The date of Henry Jr.'s death was written on a
page near the back of the Bethany Church minutes, he having died in the
same year as his father, Henry Fox.  The location is also unknown for
the graves of nne, Winnie C., and Agnes, all of whom died young.  It
is presumed that John Fox is buried in Alabama.

           DeKalb Cemeterv:  For the descendants of William Fox, Henry
and Sarah Harrell Fox s oldest son, there are two additional cemeteries
of interest.  William Fox and some of his children are buried in the old
cemetery at DeKalb, Mississippi.  We visited it in April 1961.  We could
not have found its location without the help of Mrs. Canning T. Bell
(Persia Tartt Bell), grandmother of William Stennis of DeKalb, Miss.
Mrs. Bell, then a spritely lady around eighty, went with us and climbed
through the barbed wire fence, the poison ivy vines and hedge plants,
and over the honeysuckle and stickers to show us the location, where we
uncovered their tombstones.  The cemeterY was not maintained bv th itv

                                                                        138




or any group at that time.  We found William Fox's marker (a flat stone
on the ground), and there were markers for three of William and Priscilla
Fox's children:  Mary Ann (Fox) Stovall, wife of Lewis Stovall; Julia
Fox (whose marker bore letters that appeared to be "A.B.", which tradi-
tion says stood for the college degree she received, a noteworthy achieve-
ment for a woman at that time); and for Israel W. Fox.  There was also a
marker for Mary B. Hughes Fox, first wife of William's son, Eliab Fox.

          Our directions for reaching the cemetery:  Go north one block
in the direction the Confederate Soldier on the Confederate Monument is
looking.  Turn left on the street nearest the marker, and go between the
third and fourth houses on the right hand side of the street.  (You have
to park your car and rough it from here.)  These were the homes of Hal
Overstreet and Mrs. John Little.  Climb through the fence and go up the
hill through what Mrs. Bell said was the colored section of the cemetery.
In the level space on top of the hill, go forward (through the vines--a
good idea would be to take clippers) in the direction of town, and you
should find the markers.

          Fannin:  The cemetery in which William Fox's oldest son,
Malachi Fox, and some of his family are buried is located at Fannin in
Rankin County, on the Brandon-Fannin road.  The cemetery is in the yard
of the Fannin Baptist Church.












          Fox Hunting is fun.  You meet lots of nice people.
          Years ago a friend gave me a cartoon in which one
          woman was exclaiming to another:  "Have I got
          relatives?  I've got some I'm not even related to!"

          That's a good kind, too.  If they're nice you can
          claim them and call them "Cousin"!

                                                                              143



     TO REACH FOX FAMILY CEMETERY:

(l)  From exit at Hugh White State Park into Highway 8, turn left and go east
     on Highway 8 approximately 13 miles to Grenada County-Calhoun County
     line, then continue down Highway 8 approximately 2 miles farther and
     turn right onto a hard surfaced road.

(2)  To go to the Fox Cemetery on Mr. Vardaman Springer's property, continue
     on this road to Dentontown.  The black top extends about ll miles, then
     turn right (west) onto gravel road.

(3)  Continue about 2 miles on gravel road past a white house with a sign
     "Green Meadows" and two brick houses that say "Edwards" and continue
     past several other houses to Mr. Springer's, which is a white house on
     the right side of the road.  There are several barns at right rear of
     house, and he has a semi-circular drive in front of the house.  Please
     stop to tell Mr. Springer you would like to go to the cemetery.

(4)  Drive into Mr. Springer's yard and through the gate and down pasture
     road for about a mile to where there is a big pine and big cedar on
     the right.  On the opposite side (left) there is a path  and you can
     drive part way down the trail.  Look for the best place to drive into
     the trail, depending on how it has washed.  Stop the car about 100 feet
     from where you entered the trail.  You can see the markers on a slight
     hill from this point, and you can walk up the rest of the way.

     TO REACH BETHANY CHURCH CEMETERY:

                              .

     Continue past Mr. Springer's home for about 6/10 miles to where the road
     turns sharply to the right at 45° angle just before reaching Highway 9
     intersection.  Go another 6/10 miles (past a yellow brick house and past
     a white frame house on the left, which is home of Longino Doss).  Turn
     right just beyond Mr. Doss's home onto a gravel road which dead-ends at
     Bethany Cemetery.

     TO REACH BETHEL CEMETERY:

     Follow instructions in (l) above.  Continue on the road to Dentontown,
     but do not go into Dentontown if you want the shortest route.  Turn left
     off the road about 1/4 mile before you get to Dentontown and go about 2
     miles.  The cemetery is at the intersection of that road and another road
     which meet at about 45° angles.  Cemetery is on both sides of the road,
     with the old part on the left (north) of road.  You can go on to the Fox
     Cemetery without returning to Dentontown by taking the road which angles
     back to the right and going south about 1 miles to where it intersects
     with the road leading from Dentontown.  (These three roads form a triangle.)
     Turn right onto the road from Dentontown and go about 3/4 miles on blacktop
     then turn right (west) onto gravel road.  This is the same gravel road you
     would have reached by following instructions in (2) above, and you can con-
     tinue on to the Fox Cemetery by following remainder of route shown above.
